A. 
Program 
Purpose 
and 
Project 
Development 
Objective 
1. 
Program 
purpose and 
program 
phasing: 
Program 
Purpose 
The 
main 
purpose 
of 
the 
15-year 
Adaptable 
Program 
Lending 
(APL) 
operation, 
of 
which 
the proposed 
project 
is 
Phase 
1, 
is 
essentially 
to 
assist 
the Government 
of 
Yemen 
(GOY) 
in 
slowing 
down 
depletion 
of 
groundwater 
in 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin aquifer 
to 
gain 
time 
for 
GOY 
to 
convert the 
Sana'a 
Basin 
economy 
to 
less 
water-intensive 
activities and 
encourage out-migration. 
Program 
Phasing 
The 
APL 
is 
designed 
in 
three 
phases, 
each 
of 
about 
five 
years. 
Phase 
I, 
described 
in 
detail 
in 
Section 
C, 
is 
designed 
to 
test 'best 
practice' 
technologies and 
approaches 
for 
demand 
and 
supply 
management, 
as 
well 
as 
institutional 
arrangements. 
Results 
would 
be 
closely monitored. Based 
on 
clearly defined 
'triggers' 
(see 
B-4 
- 
Triggers 
for 
Subsequent 
Credits), 
follow-on 
phases 
would 
expand 
experiences 
and 
lessons 
gained 
throughout the 
Sana'a 
Basin. 
Problem 
Statement 
Yemen, 
at 
the southern 
tip 
of 
the 
Arabian 
Peninsula, 
faces 
extreme 
water 
shortages. 
Water 
availability 
in 
Sana'a 
City, 
capital 
of 
Yemen, 
is 
one 
of 
the 
scarcest 
in 
the 
world. 
Bringing 
in 
water 
from 
outside the 
basin 
has 
been 
studied 
and 
proven 
highly 
uneconomical 
(in 
excess 
of 
US$l/m3 
to 
more 
than 
US$8/m3 
- 
desalination) 
andor 
socially 
controversial. 
If 
the 
water 
resource 
is 
to 
be 
preserved 
and 
protected 
for 
an 
extended 
life, 
there 
is 
no 
alternative 
to 
this 
project 
which 
aims 
at 
using 
water 
within 
the basin 
more 
efficiently 
by 
improveddemand 
management 
and 
increased 
recharge. 
Some 
highlights 
Hvdrologv 
The 
Sana'a 
basin 
is 
hydrologically 
self-contained, 
with 
very 
little 
outflow, 
only 
in 
years 
with 
heavy 
rainfall. Information 
on 
the 
more 
detailed 
hydrology 
of 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin 
is 
scant. 
Available 
data 
on 
storage 
and 
recharge 
stems 
and 
is 
extrapolated 
from 
studies 
and 
reports 
carried out 
and 
compiled 
in 
the 
1970's 
and 
1980's. 
Much 
of 
the data 
may 
have been 
copied 
from 
one 
report 
to 
another. 
There 
is 
no 
perennial 
surface 
water 
in 
all 
of 
Yemen. 
Annual 
average 
precipitation 
(past 
30 
years) 
is 
about 
700 
- 
800 
million 
cubic 
meters 
(MCM). 
Of 
that 
only 
about 5 percent 
infiltrates 
the 
ground 
and 
the rest 
evaporates. 
Another 
40 
MCM 
to 
80 
MCM 
seeps 
into 
the 
ground 
from 
irrigation 
and 
sewage 
return 
flows. 
The 
groundwater 
configuration 
- 
simplified 
- 
has 
two 
major 
strata: 
a 
shallow aquifer 
(alluvium 
and 
volcanic), 
with 
depths 
from 
30 
m 
to 
about 
70180 
m; 
a 
deep 
aquifer 
(Tawilah 
aquifer), 
slanting 
from 
north to 
south, 
with 
depths 
from 
less 
than 
100 
m 
to 
more 
than 
1,000 
m 
(see 
graph, 
below). 
-3- 
Page  8
Schematic Geologic 
Profile 
Sana’a 
Basin 
(verlicd 
reale 
ersggerated) 
i 
I 
I 
I 
i 
”* 
There 
is 
no 
perceptible 
recharge 
from 
the 
shallow 
to 
the 
deep 
aquifers 
(confirmed 
by 
isotope 
tests 
carried 
out 
by 
the International 
Atomic 
Energy 
Agency 
(MEA). 
Horizontal 
transmissivity 
within 
the 
Tawilah 
aquifer 
is 
estimated 
to 
be 
minimal. 
For 
practical 
purposes, 
the 
Tawilah 
water, characterized 
by 
high 
quality, 
may 
be 
considered a 
fossil 
aquifer. 
According 
to 
estimates 
derived 
from 
earlier 
studies, 
the 
economically 
exploitable 
deep 
aquifer stock 
is 
between 
2 
and 
3 
billion 
cubic 
meters 
(BCM). 
Water 
A 
bstractioniuse 
Groundwater 
use 
began 
to 
exceed recharge 
in 
the 
basin 
in 
the 
mid-1980s 
- 
the 
result 
of 
rapid 
expansion 
of 
irrigation, 
triggered 
by 
the 
introduction 
of 
the 
tubewell 
engine 
pumps 
and 
submersible 
pump 
technology 
in 
the 
seventies, 
plus 
subsidized 
diesel. 
Total 
water abstraction 
is 
about 
250 
MCM 
per 
year (about 
100 percent 
to 
150 percent 
higher 
than 
recharge). 
More 
than 
80 percent 
goes 
to 
irrigated 
agriculture 
(of 
which 
about 
half 
for 
qat 
- 
see 
below), 
with 
the 
balance 
for 
domestic 
and 
industrial 
use. 
Water 
abstraction 
is 
completely 
uncontrolled, 
unmetered, 
unlicensed 
and unpaid 
for. 
A 
recent 
well 
inventory 
(August 
2002) 
revealed 
about 
13,000 
wells 
in 
the 
Sana‘a 
Basin 
- 
and 
growing. 
Of 
these, 
only 
about 
70 
are state-owned 
for 
public 
water 
supply. 
-A- 
Page  9
A 
farmer's 
first 
choice 
is 
to 
pump 
from 
the shallow 
aquifer. Once 
it 
is 
depleted, 
and 
if 
affordable, 
he 
will 
drill 
into 
the 
Tawilah 
aquifer, hence 
depleting 
the 
drinkingquality 
water. 
Water 
OualihdSana'a 
Wastewater 
Treatment 
Plant 
('WWT 
PI 
Infiltration from 
cess 
pits 
and the 
inadequate treatment 
of 
urban 
sewage 
results 
in 
high 
levels 
of 
biological 
contamination 
in 
the 
alluvial 
aquifer and 
in 
the 
fractured 
zones 
of 
the 
volcanic aquifer 
underlying 
the 
city 
of 
Sana'a (see 
basin 
geological 
cross-section, 
above). 
The 
WWTP 
was 
completed 
in 
mid-2000, 
but 
the 
quality 
of 
the treated 
effluent 
has 
been 
inconsistent 
since  the plant's 
commissioning. 
The plant 
is 
often 
been 
bypassed, 
especially 
during 
peak flows, 
energy 'black 
- 
or 
brown-outs' 
and 
when 
oil 
and 
slaughterhouse waste 
arrives 
with 
sewer, 
releasing 
untreated 
effluent 
into 
the 
wadi to 
the 
north 
of 
the 
plant. 
Effluent, 
treated 
or 
untreated, 
is 
used 
by 
about 
600 
farmers 
for 
irrigation 
of 
about 
300 
ha 
of 
land, 
including 
for 
vegetables. 
This 
poses 
high 
risks 
of 
public 
health 
for 
both 
the 
farmers 
and 
vegetable 
consumers, 
as 
well 
as 
for 
aquifer 
contamination.  Recent 
reviews 
indicate that 
substantial 
modification 
to 
the 
WWTP 
design 
may 
be required 
to 
guarantee 
safe 
effluent 
(see 
Section 
E-5, 
below). 
Water 
Legislation 
Until 
August 
31, 
2002, 
Yemen 
had 
no 
water 
law. The 
new 
Water 
Law 
is 
an 
excellent 
step 
in 
the 
direction 
of 
improved 
water 
management, 
but 
it 
still 
allows 
water 
abstraction 
without 
license 
up 
to 
80 
m 
depth; 
it 
has 
no 
provision 
for 
water abstraction 
metering, 
nor for 
levying 
a 
water 
charge 
for 
irrigation 
water. 
Oat 
Qat, 
a 
leaf from 
a tree 
or 
bush, 
when 
chewed 
has 
stimulating 
effects. 
Qat 
is 
a 
robust 
plant 
that 
can 
go 
without 
(or 
very 
little) 
water 
for 
years 
and 
even 
survives 
droughts. 
It 
grows 
under 
similar 
altitude 
and 
climatic 
conditions 
as 
does 
coffee, 
but 
only 
in 
frost 
and 
wind 
protected 
areas 
on 
the fringes 
of 
wadis, 
not 
in 
large 
plains. 
Thus, 
qat 
expansion 
is 
naturally 
limited, 
The 
use 
of 
qat 
is 
widespread 
and 
growing. 
It 
is 
controversial 
in 
terms 
of 
its 
socio-economic  impact. 
While 
it 
is 
highly 
profitable 
for 
farmers, 
it 
may

identified four major

(i) 
Institutions

national 
debate 
on 
groundwater conservation, 
vastly 
improved public 
awareness 
and 
by 
testing 
solutions 
on 
the 
ground. 
The 
proposed 
projectlprogram 
is 
the 
key 
vehicle 
for 
pushing forward 
changes 
in 
water 
resource 
management. 
-9- 
Page  14
3. 
Sector 
issues 
to 
be 
addressed 
by 
the project and 
strategic 
choices: 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
address 
key 
sector 
issues 
on 
water resource 
management. 
(a) 
Water 
rights 
and 
regulation. 
Traditional 
water 
right 
systems 
deal adequately 
with 
surface 
water 
but 
do 
not 
provide 
a 
workable 
framework 
for 
groundwater. 
Groundwater 
exploitation 
is 
essentially 
unregulated. 
The 
owner 
of 
each 
of 
Yemen's 
50,000-100,000 
wells 
(13,000 
wells 
in 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin) 
is 
sovereign 
master 
of 
how 
much 
to 
pump. 
The 
"law 
of 
capture" prevails, 
and 
the 
incentives 
favor 
competitive 
mining 
over 
conservation. 
With 
the 
objective 
of 
changing 
pumping 
behavior, 
the 
proposed 
project 
would 
help 
draft 
and implement 
bylaws 
for 
the 
new 
Water  Law. 
As 
a 
priority, 
for 
the  'protected 
zones', 
the 
project 
would 
help 
build 
up 
monitoring 
capacity 
and 
introduce 
a 
system 
of 
self-regulation 
by 
water 
users. 
Future 
groundwater 
exploitation 
would be 
subject 
to 
licensing 
in 
advance. 
The 
new 
law 
gives 
NWRA 
these 
powers, 
but 
also 
allows 
delegation 
to 
stakeholder 
groups, 
for 
example, 
WAS 
for 
water management 
at 
the 
local 
level. 
(b) 
The 
new 
Water 
Law 
is 
key 
to 
an  effective 
water 
management program. 
It 
is 
an  important 
achievement, 
especially 
with 
water 
management 
at 
the 
center 
of 
the 
new 
CAS  for 
IDA 
interventions. 
Yet, 
the 
new 
Law 
has 
several 
significant 
flaws, 
most 
of 
which 
can 
not 
be 
simply 
'corrected' 
through 
bylaws, 
because 
the 
basic 
provisions 
are absent 
in 
the 
Law. 
These 
are 
the 
four 
key 
issues: 
(i) 
The 
Law 
does 
not 
provide 
for 
water abstraction 
measuring; 
(ii) 
The 
Law 
does 
not 
provide 
for 
levying 
of 
water 
charges; 
(iii)The 
Law 
allows 
well-diggingldrilling 
up 
to 
60 
m 
without 
a license, 
plus, 
it 
allows 
a deepening 
of 
any 
well 
by 
20 
m 
once 
(who 
is 
monitoring 
?) 
without 
a license, 
which 
makes 
effectively 
wells 
up 
to 
80 
m 
depth 
license-free; 
and 
(iv)The 
Law 
grandfathers 
all 
past 
water 
rights, 
plus 
gives 
water 
rights 
to wells 
drilled 
after  the 
effectiveness 
of 
the 
Law. 
At 
the 
same 
time, each 
well 
may 
be 
registered 
within 
three 
years 
from 
effectiveness of  the 
Law, 
thus 
encouraging 
farmers 
to 
drill 
as 
many 
wells 
as 
possible 
without 
license, 
from 
now 
to 
the 
middle 
of 
2005, 
when 
all 
wells 
have 
to 
be 
registered. 
This 
would 
increase farmers'  water 
rights, 
which 
- 
based 
on 
current abstraction 
- 
already exceed 
renewable 
resources 
by 
100 
percent 
to 
150 
percent. 
These 
shortcomings 
are 
a 
significant 
hindrance 
for 
effective 
water 
management. 
After 
consultation 
with 
IDA 
and 
other 
donors, 
GOY 
decided 
to 
declare 
under 
Article 
49 
of 
the 
Water Law, 
the 
basins 
of 
Sana'a, 
Sa'ada 
and 
Tai'iz, 
as 
'protected 
zones' 
(Cabinet 
Decrees 
# 
343 
to 
346, 
of 
November 
19, 
2002). 
This 
mitigates 
the 
law's 
flaws 
in 
the 
three 
most 
water 
scarce 
areas 
of 
the 
country.  Special 
bylaws 
will 
be 
drafted 
for 
these 
'protected 
zones' 
and 
the 
Water 
Law 
at large 
with 
IDA'S 
assistance. 
(c) 
The 
capacity 
for 
institutional 
arrangements 
to 
improved 
water 
management 
is 
insufficient 
and 
fragmented. 
Public 
institutions 
often 
lack 
authority over 
tribal 
structures and the 
strong autonomy 
of 
local 
water 
users. 
Experience 
shows 
that enforcement 
can 
only 
be 
successful 
on 
a 
partnership 
basis, 
through 
a system 
of 
self-regulation. 
The 
project 
would 
couple 
regulation 
with 
a 
participatory 
water 
resource management approach 
and 
a 
public 
information 
and 
awareness 
program 
(see 
details 
in 
Section 
C-4). 
Social 
consciousness 
is 
crucial 
for 
protecting 
the 
limited 
water resources 
of 
the 
basin 
and 
to 
engendering support 
for 
the 
regulatory activities required 
to 
conserve 
these resources. 
The project 
would, 
therefore: 
(i) 
Develoo 
NWRA's 
capacitv 
for 
water 
resources management.  Sections 
C-2 
and 
C-4 
below 
- 
10- 
Page  15
describe 
NWRA's 
future 
role 
in 
the 
proposed 
project 
in 
detail. 
Broadly, 
NWRA 
through 
a 
recently 
created 
Sana'a 
Branch, 
would 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
implementation 
and monitoring 
of 
a 
comprehensive 
regulatory 
system (bylaws) based 
on 
the 
decree 
declaring the 
Sana'a 
Basin 
as 
a 
'protected 
zone' 
(see 
above). 
The project 
would 
support 
an 
intensive 
capacity-building program 
for 
the 
NWRA 
Sana'a 
Branch. 
(ii) 
Promote 
an institutional framework 
in 
which 
the 
NWRA 
Sana'a 
Branch 
(see 
below) 
would 
delegate management 
of 
the 
resource 
to 
the most 
decentralized 
feasible 
level 
- 
to 
stakeholder 
organizations 
wherever possible. 
Peer 
enforcement and 
self-regulation 
are 
likely 
to 
be 
the best 
way 
in 
Yemeni 
conditions 
to 
ensure 
prudent water 
management. 
Stakeholder organizations 
would 
be 
created 
where 
needed. 
They 
would 
be 
built 
on 
existing 
social 
structures such 
as 
LCs, 
water 
user cooperatives, associations, 
and 
community 
organizations. 
(iii)Build 
up 
the 
capacity 
of 
the 
recently 
created 
s, 
with 
a 
long-term 
view 
to 
convert 
it 
into 
a 
Sana'a 
Basin 
Agency 
(SBA). 
(iv) 
Strengthen 
GDI's 
technical, 
financial 
and 
procurement 
capacity 
for 
its 
vital 
role 
in 
irrigation 
improvement and 
dam 
construction. 
(d) 
Save 
water 
in 
agriculture, 
and 
make 
saved 
water available 
for 
higher 
value 
uses 
in 
the 
basin. 
As 
elsewhere 
in 
Yemen, 
irrigation efficiency 
is 
low. 
Achieving project 
and 
program 
objectives 
will 
depend 
largely 
on 
water savings 
in 
irrigation. 
Techniques 
for 
irrigation 
improvement 
are 
known 
and 
have been demonstrated 
under the completed 
IDA-financed LWCP. Under 
the proposed project, the 
focus 
would 
be 
on 
forging 
a 
partnership between 
the 
GOY 
and 
farmers 
to 
increase 
the water 
use 
efficiency 
through 
modem  irrigation 
technologies, 
to 
confine  agriculture  increasingly 
to 
the 
enhanced 
use 
of 
rainfall, 
and 
to 
extract 
irrigation 
water 
from 
the 
shallow 
aquifers.  Farmers 
would 
be 
offered 
an 
incentives and Rules 
package 
to 
adopt 
improved irrigation 
technologies, 
under 
which 
they 
would 
also 
commit 
themselves 
not 
to 
expand 
their 
irrigation 
perimeters 
(C-4 
- 
Package 
of 
'Incentives 
and 
Rules').

and

triggers 
for

of

onto 
the

basin 
to

water 
conservation. 
To 
progress 
from

to 
another, 
a 'trigger' 
mechanism 
would 
be 
set 
up. 
Triggers 
would 
be OUTPUT-oriented 
to 
be meaningful 
and 
measurable. 
Performance 
trimers 
for 
Phase 
II 
of

APL: 
O&M 
for 
Demand 
and 
Supply 
Management 
functions 
well 
(adequate 
government budget 
for

maintenance). 
GOY

to 
work 
on

diesel 
subsidies, 
as

in 
the 
policy 
letter,

5, 
dated 
September

Sana'a 
WWTP 
effluent 
standards 
(treated 
WW 
and 
sludge) 
meet 
the 
Bank's 
guidelines 
for

irrigation (Pollution 
Abatement 
and 
Prevention 
Handbook). 
Two 
thirds 
(213) 
of

targeted 
areas 
are 
converted 
to 
improved 
irrigation 
by

3

of

Project 
components 
(see

Annex

for

cost 
breakdown): 
Project activities 
would 
focus 
on 
10 
sub-basins 
(out 
of 
22 
in 
the entire Basin) 
in 
four 
representative 
Basin 
zones: 
in 
the Western Area 
(southem 
Hamdan 
and 
Bani 
Matar 
districts); 
South Eastern Area 
(Bani 
Bahloul, 
eastern 
part 
of 
Sanhan 
districts);  Eastern 
Area 
(Bani 
Hushaish, 
northern 
Bani 
AI 
Harith, 
part 
of 
Khawlen 
districts); 
and 
North 
Eastem 
Area 
(Nihm, 
and 
part 
of 
Arhab 
districts) 
- 
see 
map 
for 
details. 
Small 
dam 
rehabilitation, 
according 
to 
priorities 
based 
on 
safety 
criteria, 
would 
be 
basin-wide. 
The 
areas 
were selected 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
(i) 
representativeness 
of 
the 
sub-basins; 
(ii) 
high 
chance 
of 
success 
in 
meeting 
project 
goals; 
and 
(iii) 
favorable 
environmental 
impact 
on 
the 
selected 
sub-basins, 
Le.: 
(i) 
scope 
for 
irrigation 
improvement and 
reduction 
in 
groundwater 
abstraction; 
(ii) 
favorable 
socioeconomic conditions, 
triballethnic 
homogeneity, 
and 
communities' 
willingness 
to 
participate; 
(iii) 
positive 
impact 
on 
the 
potential 
recharge; 
and 
(iv) 
scope 
for 
supply 
structures 
(dams) 
development 
and 
improvement. 
(a)  Demand 
Management 
and 
Irrigation 
Improvement 
(Total 
cost 
US$I 
1.7 
million) 
This 
component 
seeks 
to 
achieve  water  savings 
and 
conservation 
in 
agriculture 
by 
increasing the 
efficiency 
of 
water 
use 
for 
irrigation 
through 
introduction 
of 
modem irrigation 
technologies. 
The 
component 
would 
focus 
on all 
or 
part 
of 
the 
10 
selected 
sub-basins. 
However, 
out 
of 
the 
total 
12,500 
ha 
irrigated 
land 
of 
these 
sub-basins, 
the 
project 
would initially 
target  about 
4,000 
ha. 
These 
areas 
have 
proven the most 
suitable 
during 
stakeholder 
assessments 
in 
the course 
of 
project 
preparation. 
Activities 
would 
include: 
Water 
Conservation: 
Physical interventions under 
this 
component 
would 
be 
demand-driven 
with 
an incentive 
system 
(see 
C-4 
(vi), 
below). 
Improved overall irrigation 
efficiencies 
in 
the target 
area are 
expected through: 
(a) 
upgrades 
of 
existing 
piped delivery 
systems; 
(b) 
conversions 
of 
open channel 
to 
piped 
delivery 
systems; 
and 
(c) 
introduction 
of 
modem 
pressurized 
irrigation to 
fields 
already 
served 
by 
satisfactorily 
piped delivery 
systems. 
Also 
included 
may 
be 
up 
to 
300 
ha 
of 
land 
leveling, 
plastic 
tunnelslcovers 
for 
technology  demonstration 
purposes 
and 
wadi 
protection 
works 
in 
vulnerable 
areas. 
To 
reduce 
the 
risk 
of 
irrigation 
expansion,  pipes 
for 
conveyance 
systems 
would, 
wherever possible, 
be 
PVC 
rather than 
steel 
to 
make 
diversion 
of 
water 
to 
new 
areas 
more 
difficult. 
(ii) 
Communitv 
Organization: 
The project 
will 
be 
advertised 
in 
the 
selected 
sub-basins, 
and farmer 
groups 
(usually  around 
a 
well) 
interested

will

(LC)

(see 
(9 
ii, 
below). 
They

team 
([SMT] 
- 
see 
Institutional 
Arrangements) 
to

The 
SMT 
will 
help 
with

WAS. 
These 
WAS, 
the

GDI

(v) 
Within

On

about

safety, maintenance 
and 
O&M 
cost recovery.

The latter

Stakeholder

(vi)WUAs: 
Water 
user 
groups 
(WUGs)

and

(Water Law 
# 
33

31, 
2002). 
An

or well-field 
WUA

for

in 
the 
irrigation

program. 
WAS 
constitute 
official 
stakeholder representations 
to 
whom 
the 
central 
structure 
(NWRA 
Sana'a 
Branch) 
is 
expected 
to

management,

who 
would

be 
represented 
in

SBC. 
The

would

and

of

and

yet

for

(vii)

of

and

along

These

They

dam 
recharge 
capabilities 
(see

(viii,

Overlaps

and 
WUFs 
may 
occur and 
be

in

where

are

In

federate 
to form 
WUFs 
at

level. They 
would 
also 
be

as 
water 
sector stakeholders 
on 
the SBC. 
(viii)

of 
Dams:

This

and

roads

Second,

from 
recharge 
and

roads

For

and 
as 
indicated 
above,

initial 
support

in

Specialized

for

Financial

(FM)

The

The project

The

of

The

to

of 
all

FM 
System: 
Establishment 
of 
adequate 
FM 
system  acceptable 
to 
IDA 
will 
be 
a 
Condition 
of 
Efectiveness. 
The main 
elements 
of 
adequate 
FM 
system 
are: 
(i) 
qualified financial 
officer; 
(ii) 
FM 
procedural 
manual; 
(iii) 
accounting 
records 
and 
books 
of 
accounts; 
(iv) 
appointment 
of 
qualified 
independent 
auditor;  and 
(v) 
set 
up 
of 
computerized  accounting 
and 
reporting 
system  capable 
of 
producing 
Financial 
Monitoring 
Reports 
(FMRs). 
Completion 
of 
the 
first 
four 
components 
in 
a manner 
satisfactory 
to 
IDA 
would 
be sufficient 
to 
meet the 
Condition 
of 
Effectiveness. 
To 
expedite 
the process 
of 
establishment 
of 
adequate 
FM 
system, 
the 
TS-SBC 
will 
benefit 
from 
FM 
experiences 
gained 
through 
previous 
IDA-financed 
projects 
(Le. 
Rural 
Access  Project), 
through 
the 
adaptation 
of 
existing 
FM 
manuals 
to 
the specific 
needs 
of 
this 
project 
by 
the 
project 
financial 
officer. 
Therefore,  the 
appointment 
of 
qualified financial 
officer, 
which 
will 
take 
place 
shortly 
after 
signing 
the 
credit 
agreement 
according 
to 
TORS 
acceptable 
to 
IDA, 
is 
a 
key 
step 
toward 
having 
adequate 
FM 
system. 
The 
computerized 
FM 
system 
will 
be 
based 
on 
local 
FM 
software 
to 
be 
selected 
from 
the several

shelf 
software applications 
available 
on

local 
market.

the 
Central 
Bank 
of 
Yemen 
(CBY) 
and 
managed 
solely 
by 
the 
TS-SBC 
(see 
Annex 
6-B). 
Withdrawals 
from 
IDA 
funds 
will 
be 
made 
using 
traditional 
disbursement 
methods, 
including 
direct 
payments, 
payments 
against 
special 
commitments, reimbursements 
and 
replenishments 
to 
the Special 
Account 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
Statement 
of 
Expenditures 
(SOEs) 
and 
full 
documentation, 
as 
required. 
GOY’S 
share 
of 
the 
project 
cost 
will 
flow 
through 
a 
local 
currency 
account 
at 
the 
CBY. 
Also, 
a 
separate 
project 
account 
will 
be 
established 
at 
a

bank 
or 
the 
CACB 
for 
contributions 
received 
from 
project 
beneficiaries 
and 
WUAs 
(Demand 
Management 
component), 
and 
disbursement 
from 
this 
account 
will 
be 
made 
by 
the 
TS-SBC 
to 
support 
project 
costs 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
project’s 
internal control 
procedures. Payments 
to 
contractors 
and 
-

third 
parties 
from

currently

Auditing:

project financial 
statements 
(balance 
sheet,

and 
cash 
flow 
statements) 
will 
be 
subject 
to 
annual

in 
accordance 
with 
International 
Standards 
on 
Auditing 
and 
TORS 
acceptable 
to 
IDA. 
The

will 
cover

records 
and 
accounts, 
including 
audit 
of 
the 
Special 
Account, 
GOY’s 
share 
local

and the beneficiaries’ 
and 
WUA 
contributions 
deposit account, 
Also, 
the

will 
cover

expenditures 
with 
particular attention 
to 
those expenditures made 
under the

The

by

acceptable 
to

selected 
under 
selection 
procedures 
applicable 
to

Procurement 
The

would 
be

of

project. 
The project 
would 
provide 
training 
and 
TA 
for 
the 
required 
staffing 
of 
the 
line 
ministry 
(MAI), 
so 
that eventually 
it 
will 
be 
capable 
to 
cany 
out 
these 
activities 
autonomously 
(see 
above). 
Details 
on 
Procurement 
and 
FM 
are 
explained 
in 
Annex 
6. 
D. 
Project 
Rationale 
1. 
Project 
alternatives 
considered 
and 
reasons 
for 
rejection: 
Three alternatives were 
considered: 
(i) 
Imvorting 
Water 
from 
Outside 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin. 
This 
alternative 
would 
not 
only 
be 
expensive 
and 
in 
the 
long-term most 
likely 
unaffordable 
(see 
Section 
A-l), 
but 
it 
would 
also 
entail 
high 
risks 
of 
political 
and 
social conflicts,  especially 
for 
transfers 
from 
areas 
where water 
is 
already 
heavily 
used. 
Such 
conflicts 
over water 
have 
occurred 
elsewhere, 
notably 
in 
Tai 
’z 
(Tai’iz 
Water 
Supply 
Project). 
This 
led 
to 
the 
conclusion 
that 
the 
most 
viable 
approach 
in 
the self-contained 
Sana’a 
Basin 
is 
relying 
on 
water available 
within 
the basin, 
by: 
(a) 
improving irrigation 
efficiency,  thereby reducing 
the gap 
between abstraction 
and 
recharge; 
and 
(b) 
accelerating 
recharge 
of 
precipitation 
(Section 
A-1). 
(ii) 
Top-down 
Avvroach to 
Water 
Resource Management. 
Community 
involvement 
in 
water  resource 
management 
is 
difficult 
and 
time 
consuming, 
and 
outcomes 
may differ from 
what 
planners 
predict. 
Hence, 
a 
more top-down 
approach was considered 
for 
the 
proposed project, 
It 
was 
rejected 
due 
to 
findings 
during 
project 
preparation, 
from 
sector work, 
other 
projects 
and 
from 
experiences elsewhere 
in 
Yemen: 
(a) 
in 
Yemen, 
like 
in 
other countries, 
local 
communal institutions 
have 
a 
good 
success 
potential 
for 
groundwater 
management 
because 
of 
vested 
interests 
of 
users 
from 
a 
common 
source; 
(b) 
water 
management 
institutions 
have 
to 
include, 
in 
one 
form or 
another, 
all 
those 
who 
have 
water 
rights 
and, 
therefore,  an 
interest 
in 
water  management 
(water 
rights, 
perceived 
or 
real, 
are 
often 
fiercely 
defended); 
and 
(c) 
a 
‘topdown’ 
centrallofficial 
institution may 
have 
some 
impact 
as 
an 
‘umbrella’ 
or 
framework 
for 
regulation, 
but 
only 
if 
it 
responds 
to 
the 
demands 
of 
broad-based 
stakeholder organizations. 
(iii) 
Business 
as 
Usual. 
A 
preliminary 
socio-economic  analysis 
conducted 
during 
project 
preparation 
compared the economic 
merits 
of 
business 
as 
usual 
(no 
action) 
with 
a 
progradproject 
aiming 
at 
increasing 
recharge 
and 
improving 
water 
use 
efficiency. The 
analysis, based 
on 
current 
rough 
and 
unproven 
assumptions 
of 
an economically 
accessible 
groundwater 
storage 
of 
between 
2 
BCM 
and 
3 
-21 
- 
Page  26
BCM 
(using 
standard 
discount 
values) 
and 
on 
the 
social 
and 
political 
difficulties 
of 
transferring 
water 
from 
one 
area 
to 
another (example:  Tai'z), 
led 
to 
the 
conclusion 
that: 
(a) 
the 
project 
could 
considerably 
extend the estimated 
life 
of 
the 
aquifers; 
(b) 
such 
an 
extension 
would 
allow 
the 
GOY 
to 
plan 
for  measures 
to 
change 
water-use 
behaviors and 
patterns 
in 
the 
basin; 
and 
(c) 
linear long-term 
projections 
usually 
result 
in 
overly 
pessimistic 
pictures. 
Unpredictable 
long-term 
social 
behavior 
creates 
socio-environmental 
dynamics 
that 
tend 
to 
favor 
survival 
rather 
than 
doomsday scenarios. 
As 
a 
consequence, 
the 
longer 
the 
time 
to 
full 
economic exhaustion 
of 
the 
aquifers, 
the better 
the 
chances 
that 
a 
socially 
acceptable 
solution 
for 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin population may 
be 
found. Hence, 
the 
project 
team 
has 
decided 
to 
proceed 
with 
the proposed 
programi'project. 
Sector Issue 
(vi) 
APL 
vs. 
Conventional 
Sector 
Investment Credit. 
The 
water 
sector, 
particularly 
viable 
water 
resource 
management, 
is 
key 
for 
the 
future 
economic 
development 
of 
Yemen. 
The 
GOY 
and 
IDA 
have 
decided 
(see 
CAS) 
that 
IDA 
would 
provide long-term 
support 
to 
this 
sector. 
The 
SBWM 
Project 
is 
a 
multi-faceted 
integrated 
water resource management 
operation that 
would 
address 
and 
test 
complex 
social 
and 
technical 
issues 
(water 
rights, 
participatory 
water 
managemen~re~latio~enforcement; 
publicflocal 
partnerships, 
novel 
demand management 
and 
recharge technologies) 
requiring 
long-term 
monitoring 
in 
order 
to 
define 
'best practices' 
in 
the 
Yemeni 
context. 
Integrated 
basin 
management 
is 
also a 
first 
for 
Yemen. Hence, 
it 
was 
decided 
at 
the 
PCD 
stage 
that 
an 
APL 
approach 
would 
be 
best 
suited 
for 
this 
long-term 
support 
and 
for 
drawing 
lessons 
that 
may 
be 
applied 
to 
other basins 
in 
Yemen. 
Latest 
Supervision 
(Bank-financed 
projects 
only) 
Project 
(PSR)

Lessons 
learned and 
reflected 
in 
the 
project 
design: 
From 
the 
Taiz Water 
Supply 
and 
Sanitation 
Project 
The small 
Taiz 
Water Supply 
and 
Sanitation 
Pilot 
Project 
has 
a 
water 
resource management component 
that 
addresses 
issues 
of 
stakeholder 
involvement 
in 
resource management 
and water 
conservation. 
The 
implementation 
of 
the 
project 
has 
been 
difficult. 
Project implementation 
progress was 
finally 
up-graded 
to 
”satisfactory”, 
while 
meeting 
Development  Objectives 
was  ”unsatisfactory”. 
The 
project 
has 
been 
completed and 
the 
ICR 
has 
been finalized. 
Useful 
lessons 
for 
this 
project, include: 
(i) 
An 
up-front good 
understa~ding 
of 
the 
water 
resource, 
the 
shape 
of 
the 
aquifers 
and 
the 
relation 
between 
them, 
and 
the 
relationships 
between surface 
and 
groundwater 
resources 
is 
helpful 
for 
identifying 
the 
“hydraulic 
goal” 
of 
the 
project, 
i.e., 
the concept 
of 
sustainability 
of 
an aquifer 
andor 
its 
‘acceptable’ 
rate 
of 
depletion. 
But 
in 
Yemen, 
this 
is 
in 
most 
cases 
not 
well 
known. 
(ii) 
A 
clear 
water 
resource 
management 
strategy 
is 
required 
to 
define 
how 
the 
hydraulic goal 
is 
to 
be 
achieved, 
what the 
role 
of 
each 
actor 
is, 
and 
what 
is 
the 
package 
of 
rules and 
incentives 
which 
are expected 
to 
bring 
about 
changes 
in 
water management 
behavior. 
(iii) 
A 
stakeholder analysis 
should be carried out 
that: 
(a) 
identifies 
rights 
holders, the 
nature 
of 
their 
rights 
and 
their 
interests; 
(b) 
identifies 
other 
users 
and 
their 
rights, 
interests 
and 
relation 
to 
the 
water 
rights 
holders; 
(c) 
analyses 
the 
existing 
organizational 
and 
institutional 
structure, 
including 
the 
rules 
and 
sanctions; 
and 
(d) 
conducts  a 
“socio-political” 
assessment 
of 
the 
dynamics 
and 
the 
scope 
for 
change. 
(iv) 
The 
enabling~amework 
for 
decentralized 
management 
is 
crucial 
for 
the 
success 
of 
a 
project. 
This 
means: 
(a) 
allowing local 
organizations 
to 
handle 
their 
own 
affairs; 
(b) 
recognizing 
and 
respecting 
traditional 
rights 
and 
the 
decisions 
of 
decentralized institutions; 
and 
(c) 
providing 
needed tools, rules, education, 
information, 
technology, incentives, a 
“court 
of 
appeal”. 
From 
the 
Land 
and Water 
Conservation 
Project 
(LWCP) 
The 
principal 
relevant 
lessons 
from 
LWCP 
are, 
first, 
that 
it 
is 
possible 
to 
reap 
substantial water 
savings 
in 
irrigation 
by 
the 
introduction 
of 
piped 
conveyance 
and 
distribution 
systems, 
using 
buried 
PVC 
pipes. 
About 
550 
ha 
of 
these 
systems 
have  been 
installed 
in 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin 
under 
LWCP. 
Localized 
irrigation 
improvements,  such 
as 
drip 
and 
bubbler 
technologies,  have also 
been 
piloted 
under 
LWCP. 
The results 
of 
these 
pilots 
have 
been 
mixed, 
but 
recent analysis 
suggests 
that 
in 
practice 
overall 
irrigation 
efficiency may 
be 
increased 
by 
these 
means 
from 
the present 
35 
percent 
to 
about 
60 
percent. 
A 
second 
lesson 
is 
that farmers 
are 
interested 
in 
taking 
up 
the costs 
of 
these 
improvements, 
but 
generally 
only 
if 
a 
measure 
of 
subsidy 
(under 
LWCP 
about 
30-40 
percent) 
is 
provided. 
The 
economic 
analysis  shows 
positive 
benefits 
when 
water 
is 
valued 
at 
opportunity 
cost (measured 
as 
the 
return 
to 
water 
in 
the highest 
value available 
use), 
but 
most farmers 
do 
not pay 
the 
opportunity 
cost 
for 
water. Subsidies 
are 
therefore 
needed 
- 
as 
an 
interim 
measure, 
combined 
with 
an 
intensive 
awareness 
campaign 
- 
to 
progressively 
persuade farmers 
to 
change 
their 
pumping 
behavior. 
Although 
the 
incentive 
system 
has 
shown 
positive 
results 
in 
reducing 
pumping, 
a 
final 
lesson 
is 
that 
there 
is 
no 
guarantee 
that  water 
saved 
will 
not 
be 
reused 
by 
the 
same 
farmer 
or 
neighboring 
farmers 
to 
expand 
cultivation. 
There was 
no 
enforcement 
to 
stop 
irrigation 
expansion 
under 
the 
LWCP. 
The 
proposed 
project 
would 
require 
contracts 
(to 
be 
enforced 
by 
community 
management 
institutions) 
to 
ban 
irrigation 
expansion 
(see 
C-1 
Demand 
Management; 
C-4 
- 
Institutional 
Arrangements). 
- 
23 
- 
Page  28
4. 
Indications 
of 
borrower 
commitment 
and ownership: 
As 
mentioned 
in 
Section 
B 
- 
2 
- 
Main 
Sector 
Issues 
and Government 
Strategy, 
IDA 
has 
been 
working 
with 
GOY 
since the mid-1990s 
to 
reform 
and modernize 
the 
water 
sector. 
This 
resulted 
in 
a 1997 
IDA 
Water 
Strategy, 
the 
gist 
of 
which 
was adopted 
by 
the 
Council 
of 
Ministers 
in 
December  1998 
as 
the 
Yemeni 
Water 
Strategy. 
This 
strategy 
encompasses 
among 
other 
approaches, 
integrated 
water resource 
management. 
At 
the request 
of 
the 
GOY, 
IDA 
started 
preparing 
the 
SBWM 
Project 
in 
1999. 
Further 
indications 
of 
commitment 
and 
ownership include: 
(i) 
Approving 
the Water 
Law 
(although 
the 
Law 
has 
serious  shortcomings), 
and 
declaring  the 
water-scarcest 
areas 
of 
the 
country, the basins 
of 
Sana'a, 
Sa'ada 
and 
Tai'iz, 
under 
Article 
49 
of 
the 
Water 
Law 
as 
'protected 
zones 
(see 
B-3 
above). 
(ii) 
Beginning 
of 
work 
by 
NWRA 
on 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin Master 
Plan. 
(iii) 
Setting 
up 
a 
Supervisory Committee under 
the 
MOPD 
to 
supervise 
project 
preparation. 
(iv) 
Active 
participation 
of 
MAUGDI. 
(v) 
Creation 
of 
SBC 
under  the 
chairmanship 
of 
Mayor 
of 
Sana'a 
City, 
pointing 
the 
direction 
towards water 
management 
through basin 
management agencies country-wide. 
5. 
Value 
added 
of 
Bank 
support 
in 
this 
project: 
The 
IDA'S 
long-standing 
involvement 
in 
the water sector 
in 
Yemen 
puts 
it 
at 
the 
forefront 
for 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin 
water 
management 
reform 
agenda. 
IDA 
is 
by 
far, 
GOY'S 
principal 
partner 
in 
water 
resources 
management 
and 
strategy. 
GOY, 
IDA 
and 
other  donors 
worked 
together 
on 
the 
1997 
Water 
Sector 
Review, 
and 
IDA 
assisted 
in 
the 
development 
and 
discussion 
of 
the 
National 
Water 
Strategy, 
approved 
by 
the Cabinet 
(see 
above). 
Also, 
the 
IDA 
has 
been 
financing 
and 
intensively 
supervising 
the 
Taiz 
Water 
Supply 
Pilot 
Project 
since 
1996, 
which 
was 
testing 
approaches 
to 
integrated water 
resource management 
and 
producing 
lessons that 
will 
be 
of 
value 
for 
implementing the 
SBWM 
progradproject 
(see 
D-3). 
Finally, 
IDA 
has: 
(i) 
worldwide 
experience 
with 
integrated water 
resource management; 
(ii) 
technical 
resources 
that 
can 
bring 
international 
expertise 
to 
bear 
on 
the 
complex 
problems 
of: 
(a) 
groundwater 
management 
under 
severe 
drought  and 
overdraft 
conditions; 
(b) 
institutional 
arrangements that have 
worked 
elsewhere; 
and 
(c) 
water 
legislation 
and

and 
(iii)the 
respect 
of 
other donors 
in 
Yemen

elsewhere) 
to

in

to 
help 
GOY 
to 
come 
to 
grips 
with 
groundwater 
management.

on

unknown. 
A

Net

(NWS)

8

7

the

3. 
Technical: 
Water 
Resources 
and 
Hydrogeological 
Assessment 
The aquifer 
system 
in 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin 
is 
complex and 
our knowledge 
is 
limited. 
Thus, 
the analysis 
of 
the 
groundwater 
balance 
focuses 
on 
the 
consumptive 
use 
versus 
active 
replenishment. 
Estimates 
of 
the 
net 
consumptive 
use (after 
deduction 
of 
recharge 
from 
reflow) 
range 
from 
155 
MCM 
to 
190 
MCM. 
Recharge 
from 
precipitation 
is 
estimated 
from 
40 
MCM 
to 
65 
MCM. 
The 
deficit, 
or 
mining 
of 
aquifer 
storage, 
is 
therefore 
between 
90 
MCM 
and 150 
MCM 
per 
year. 
While 
the 
recharge 
effect 
on 
shallow 
aquifers 
in 
some 
areas 
seems 
to 
be significant 
(Thoma, 
Allujima 
and Al-Jeaf 
dams), 
even 
with 
widespread 
agricultural 
real 
water 
saving 
(introduction 
of 
modern 
irrigation 
technologies) 
and 
intensified 
artificial 
wadi 
recharge, 
it 
is 
unlikely 
that 
a 
water 
balance can 
be 
restored 
in 
the 
basin. Hence, the strategy 
is 
to 
reduce the 
speed 
of 
mining 
and 
extend 
the 
useful 
life 
of 
the 
aquifers 
to 
gain time 
for 
GOY 
to 
find 
long-term 
solutions, 
for 
example, 
in 
shifting 
the focus 
of 
the 
basin' 
s 
economy 
to 
less 
water 
intensive activities 
and 
to 
encourage 
out-migration. 
During 
project 
implementation, 
more 
accurate 
figures 
on 
actual 
depletionhecharge rates 
of 
the 
deep 
Tawilah 
aquifer, 
as 
well 
as 
of 
the shallow 
ones, 
are 
expected 
to 
be 
obtained through 
exploration 
and 
monitoring 
wells. 
Furthermore, 
water transfer 
options 
beyond 
those 
studied under 
SAWAS may 
also 
have 
to 
be 
explored, 
notably 
those 
in 
little 
populated 
areas 
like 
the 
groundwater 
resources 
of 
the 
desert 
area 
of 
Ramlat 
Assaba'atain. 
Wastewater 
Rechargemeuse 
The 
option 
of 
recharging 
wastewater 
from 
the 
Sana'a 
urban 
area has 
been considered 
@resent average 
about  7 
MCM 
per 
year), 
but 
was discarded 
because: 
(a) 
the 
effluent 
is 
currently 
used 
by 
about 600 
farmers 
downstream 
from 
the 
treatment 
plant, 
who 
are 
using 
it 
to 
irrigate 
about 
300 
ha 
of 
land 
and 
taking 
it 
away 
from 
them 
may 
create 
unrest; 
(b) 
artificial 
recharge 
requires 
an 
effluent quality 
assurance 
(to 
prevent  aquifer 
pollution) 
that  can 
not 
be 
guaranteed; 
and 
(c) 
artificial 
recharge  technologies 
are 
sophisticated, 
requiring 
well-trained 
staff 
and 
may 
have 
high 
operating 
costs 
(energy). 
Recharging 
naturally 
from 
open ponds 
would 
not 
be 
feasible 
or 
practical 
because 
farmers 
would 
inevitably 
use the 
water 
in 
ponds 
for 
irrigation, 
plus 
evaporation 
rates 
in 
this 
dry 
highland 
climate 
are 
too 
high 
to 
justify 
the 
investment. 
RurallUrban Water 
Transfers 
The 
difficulty 
of 
arranging equitable 
and sustainable 
rural-urban 
transfers 
has 
been 
at the 
heart 
of 
the 
urban 
water 
supply 
problem 
for 
years. 
GOY 
has 
tended 
to 
assert 
national 
ownership 
and 
to 
appropriate 
the 
resource 
by 
drilling 
deeper 
for 
urban 
use 
than 
other 
users, 
but 
this 
has 
contributed 
to 
competitive 
over-pumping 
of 
aquifers. 
Competition 
for 
water 
has become 
so 
intense 
that  communities have 
even 
opposed  test 
drilling 
(Tai'iz 
Water 
Supply 
Pilot 
Project). 
Official 
water 
markets 
are 
not 
allowed. 
Unofficial 
water 
transfers 
exist 
everywhere 
through 
the 
sale 
of 
water 
in 
tanker trucks 
from 
agriculture 
wells 
to 
domestic users (about 
two-thirds 
of 
all 
water 
supplied 
in 
Sana'a 
comes 
from 
private 
sources 
- 
two 
private 
distribution 
networks 
and 
tanker  trucks). 
However, 
an 
open discussion 
of 
rural 
- 
urban 
transfer 
would 
not 
be 
well-received 
by 
the 
farming community 
at 
this 
point. 
This 
has 
been 
clearly 
demonstrated 
during 
stakeholder meetings 
in 
the course 
of 
project 
preparation. 
- 
27

Page  32
A 
different  approach 
is 
proposed. 
Initially, 
water 
not 
abstracted 
for 
irrigation 
from 
the 
Tawilah 
aquifer 
which 
primarily 
serves 
Sana'a 
City 
and 
adjacent 
suburbia, 
is 
water  saved 
for 
later 
municipal 
use. 
Therefore, 
making 
more 
water  available 
from 
shallow 
depths 
for 
agricultural 
purposes 
is 
a 
win-win 
situation.  Farmers 
save 
on 
pumping 
costs 
and 
urban 
dwellers 
benefit 
from 
a 
longer 
life 
of 
the 
deep, 
largely unpolluted 
aquifer. 
In 
the longer 
run, 
and 
as 
abstraction 
regulations, 
water 
rights 
and 
well 
metering 
take 
hold, 
in 
combination 
with 
improved irrigation 
technologies 
and an 
intense 
awareness campaign, 
it 
may 
be 
possible 
that, 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
year, 
well 
owners 
find 
they 
have 
used 
less 
water 
than 
their 
allowed 
quota. 
This 
may 
create a 
farmer-driven 
market 
to 
sell 
excess 
water 
to 
urban 
areas. 
A 
savvy government 
may 
then 
play 
a 
supportive 
role 
in 
organizing 
and 
regulating rural-urban 
water 
trade. 
4. 
Institutional: 
Water 
Law 
As 
mentioned 
in 
B-3 
above, 
the 
Water 
Law 
had 
been 
approved, 
but 
it 
has 
significant 
flaws. However, 
in 
the 
country's water-scarcest 
areas, 
the 
basins 
of 
Sana'a,  Sa'ada 
and 
Tai'iz, 
these 
flaws 
have been 
mitigated 
by 
Cabinet 
Decrees 
declaring 
the 
areas, 
under 
Article 
49 
of 
the 
Water Law, 
as 
'protected 
zones'. 
Bylaws 
for 
the 
Water 
Law 
in 
general 
and 
for 
the 
'protected 
zones' 
in 
particular 
will 
be drafted shortly 
with 
assistance 
from 
IDA. 
Water 
Use 
Rights 
The 
law 
addresses 
water 
rights, 
but 
may 
in 
the 
short 
run 
encourage 
more 
well-drilling, 
thereby increasing 
water 
rights 
which 
- 
already 
today 
- 
exceeds 
renewable capacity 
by 
100 
percent 
to 
150 
percent. 
The 
'protected 
zones' 
decree 
would 
help 
alleviate  over-pumping 
in 
the 
critical 
areas, 
including 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin 
(Section B-3, 
above). 
Package 
of 
Incentives 
and 
Rules 
To 
change 
water 
management 
behavior, a package 
of 
incentives 
and 
rules 
for 
water 
users 
in 
the 
basin 
is 
proposed. 
Irrigation 
equipment 
would 
be 
provided 
under 
a cost-sharing 
agreement 
(subsidy) 
(see 
C-4 
- 
Institutional 
Arrangements) 
provided 
farmers reduce 
pumping 
from 
the 
deep 
aquifer and do 
not 
expand 
irrigated 
areas. 
Dams 
would 
be 
built 
and 
rehabilitated 
under 
a 
contract 
with 
the 
beneficiaries, 
stipulating 
that 
they 
take 
care 
of 
O&M, 
with 
a cost-sharing agreement 
between 
govemment 
(GDI) 
and 
beneficiaries 
to 
be 
phased 
out over 
the 
project 
period 
(C-4, 
above). 
This 
approach 
is 
consistent 
with 
that 
used 
in 
programs 
worldwide 
for 
changing 
individual 
resource management behavior, 
where 
the 
public 
benefit 
greatly 
exceeds 
the 
private 
benefit, 
and 
where the 
costs 
are 
bome 
largely 
by 
individuals, 
but 
the 
benefits 
are 
felt 
by 
the 
population 
at 
large. 
Typically, 
subsidies 
of 
various 
kinds 
are 
applied 
in 
return 
for 
agreement 
to 
comply 
with 
certain 
rules. 
Experience 
also shows 
that at the 
end 
of 
the subsidized investment 
phase 
such 
programs can 
deteriorate, 
unless there 
is 
a 
continuing 
flow 
of 
benefits 
andor 
unless a 
pattem 
of 
respect 
for 
new 
rules can be 
developed. 
Yemeni 
experience 
with 
this 
approach 
is 
very 
limited, 
and 
outcomes 
are 
difficult to 
predict. 
Therefore, 
close 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
would 
be 
essential. 
4.1 
Executing 
agencies: 
NWRA 
has 
national responsibilities, 
but 
limited 
capacity 
and, 
therefore, 
its 
active 
role 
in 
the 
project 
would 
be 
limited. 
Consequently, 
the 
creation 
of 
a 
NWRA 
Sana'a 
Branch 
is 
proposed. 
With 
strong 
support 
-28- 
Page  33
(capacity 
building, 
training, 
TA) 
from 
the project, 
it 
is 
expected that  the 
NWRA 
Sana’a 
Branch 
will 
become a 
viable 
regulatory 
and 
planning 
agency 
for 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin. (C-4 
- 
Institutional 
Arrangements). 
4.2 
Project 
management: 
See 
above 
and 
also 
C-4 
- 
Institutional 
Arrangement. 
4.3 
Procurement 
issues: 
The 
TS-SBC 
will 
have a 
procurement 
officer 
among 
its 
staff. 
Intensive 
training 
is 
foreseen 
to 
build 
up 
the 
TS-SBC‘s 
procurement capacity 
in 
the 
first 
year. 
A 
procurement 
plan 
for 
the 
first 
year was agreed 
upon 
during 
negotiations. 
A 
full 
procurement 
capacity 
assessment 
would 
be 
carried out 
once  the 
TS-SBC 
is 
fully 
operational. 
More 
detailed procurement 
methods 
and 
thresholds are presented 
in 
Annex 
6. 
4.4 
Financial 
management 
issues: 
The 
FM 
arrangements 
to 
be used 
in 
the 
project 
would 
be 
centralized 
at 
the 
TS-SBC 
within 
the 
SBC. 
The 
identified 
risk 
areas 
are 
assessed 
as 
High 
due to: 
(a) 
the 
complexity 
of 
the 
project 
arrangements; 
(b) 
the 
involvement 
of 
several government 
agencies 
and 
local 
WAS 
in 
the 
implementation 
process; 
(c) 
local 
contributions 
from 
beneficiaries  and 
WUAs, 
which 
require 
close 
monitoring 
and 
detailed accounting 
procedures; 
and 
(d) 
the  TS-SBC 
financial 
officer 
and other 
staff 
would 
be 
newly 
recruited 
without 
relevant 
experience 
in 
IDA-financed  project. 
These 
risks 
would 
be 
mitigated 
through: 
(a) 
the 
creation 
of 
a 
fully 
staffed 
TS-SBC, 
with 
a 
qualified financial 
officer, 
would 
allow 
the 
necessary segregation 
of 
duties 
according 
to 
the 
generally 
accepted 
internal 
control 
rules; 
(b) 
expedite 
the 
establishment 
of 
an 
FM 
manual through 
adaptation 
of 
the 
FM 
manual 
of 
the 
Rural 
Access 
Project 
to 
the 
specific 
needs 
of 
this 
project 
(time 
and 
cost 
savings); 
(c) 
a 
well-defined 
role 
of 
the 
MOF 
and 
MOPD 
in 
the disbursement 
approval 
stage; 
(d) 
intensive 
reviews 
by 
IDA 
staff,  for 
both 
procurement 
and 
SOEs, 
would 
strengthen 
the 
internal 
control 
and 
minimize 
the inherent 
risks 
associated 
with 
SOEs; 
and 
(e) 
close 
supervision 
by 
IDA’S 
staff. 
5. 
Environmental: 
5.1 
Summarize the 
steps 
undertaken 
for 
environmental 
assessment 
and 
EMP 
preparation 
(including 
consultation 
and 
disclosure) 
and 
the 
significant 
issues 
and 
their 
treatment 
emerging 
from 
this 
analysis. 
This 
project 
is 
an 
environmental  improvement 
operation. 
It 
has 
been 
classified 
as 
an 
environment 
Category 
‘A’ 
intervention 
because 
of 
the 
proposed  construction 
of 
two 
small 
hill 
dams 
(recharge 
structures) 
that 
exceed 
15 
meters 
of 
height 
(16 
m 
and 
18 
m), 
the 
potential 
social 
issues 
related 
to 
water 
management 
in 
an 
extremely water 
scarce 
environment, 
and 
the 
overall complexity 
of 
the project. 
This 
triggered 
a 
full 
EIA 
(OP 
4.01) 
and 
an 
EMP. 
Environmental 
Category: 
A 
(Full 
Assessment) 
The draft 
EM 
and 
EMP 
have 
been 
prepared 
and distributed 
to 
the 
InfoShop 
on 
March 
7, 
2003. 
The 
document 
was 
also 
disclosed 
to 
the 
public 
in 
Yemen 
on 
March 
10,2003 
through 
the 
World 
Bank 
office 
in 
Sana’a, 
the 
Water 
and 
Environment 
Center 
(WEC) 
of 
the 
Sana’a 
University, 
the 
Public Library, 
the 
Environmental Protection 
Authority 
(EPA), 
as 
well 
as 
the 
Office 
of 
the 
Project 
Preparation 
Team. 
Both, 
the 
full 
EIA 
and 
the 
Executive 
Summary 
with 
the 
EMP 
are 
also 
available 
in 
the 
Project 
File. 
The 
EL4 
was 
carried 
out 
on 
behalf 
of 
GOY, 
under 
the leadership 
of 
an 
intemational 
environmental 
specialist, 
with 
four 
local 
experts 
in 
pest 
management, 
cultural 
heritage, 
biodiversity, and 
public 
health. 
The 
EL4 
drew 
data 
from 
ongoing project preparation 
studies, 
satellite 
imagery, 
social 
assessment, 
rapid 
rural 
appraisal, 
wells 
and 
dams 
inventories 
and 
several 
field 
visits. 
It 
also 
assessed 
a 
number 
of 
institutions 
at 
both 
national 
and 
local 
levels, 
governmental 
and 
non-governmental, 
which would 
or 
might 
be 
involved 
in 
the 
project 
and 
its 
social and environmental 
complexity. The 
main 
counterparts 
for 
this 
-29- 
Page  34
project 
would 
be 
the 
NWRA 
Sana‘a 
Branch, 
WGDI 
and the 
DPP, 
which 
would 
play 
a 
key role 
in 
implementing 
the 
PMP. 
The 
MOPHP would 
closely 
monitor 
the 
incidence 
of 
schistosomiasis 
in 
the 
project 
area. 
The 
EIA 
evaluated 
the 
Bank’s 
Environmental 
and 
Social 
Safeguard 
Policies 
for 
possible application, 
Particularly reviewed 
were 
“Natural  Habitat”  (OPIBP 
4.04);  “Pest 
Management” 
(OP 
4.09); 
“Cultural 
Property” 
(OPN 
1 
1.03); 
“Involuntary 
Resettlement” 
which 
includes 
Involuntary 
Land 
Acquisition 
(OPIBP 
4.12); 
and 
“Safety 
of 
Dams”  (OPIBP 
4.37). 
Except 
for 
Dam 
Safety, 
Involuntary 
Resettlement 
(for 
land 
acquisition) 
and 
Pest 
Management, 
the 
Bank’s 
Safeguard 
Policies 
are 
not 
triggered. 
Related 
issues 
and 
mitigation 
measures 
are 
described 
below. 
Dam 
Safetv 
(i) 
The 
rehabilitation 
of 
existing 
dams 
proposed 
for 
this 
project 
is 
a 
mitigation 
measure 
to 
address 
safety 
hazards. 
Out 
of 
44 
dams 
in 
the 
basin, 
24 
were 
assessed 
as 
needing 
rehabilitation. 
Out 
of 
these, 
11 
dams 
throughout the basin 
are 
considered 
priorities 
and 
included 
in 
the proposed project, 
as 
they 
may 
pose a serious safety 
risk 
for 
downstream 
populations 
and 
require 
immediate 
work. 
(ii) 
Out 
of 
the 
nine 
sites 
for 
new 
dams 
identified, 
the 
project 
team 
selected 
five 
recharge 
structures, 
two 
of 
which 
exceed 
15 meters, 
two 
smaller 
dams 
and 
a cascading series 
of 
check 
structures. 
In 
addition, 
other 
smaller 
surface 
retention, 
or 
sub-surface 
structures 
will 
be 
tested 
during 
project 
implementation. 
The 
selection 
of 
the 
dam 
sites was  based 
on 
a 
rapid 
social 
assessment 
which 
determined 
the 
level 
of 
community 
demand, 
or 
willingness 
to 
participate. 
Observing 
the 
demand-responsiveness 
principle, 
only 
sites 
with 
the highest favorable 
rating 
were 
selected. 
A 
dam 
safety 
expert 
visited 
all 
sites 
and 
preliminarily 
concluded that the 
sites 
and 
foreseen 
work 
for 
the 
selected 
dams, 
both 
rehabilitation 
and 
new, 
would 
not 
pose 
any safety 
risk. 
However, he 
will 
also 
review 
feasibility 
studies, 
final 
designs 
and 
bidding 
documents. 
In 
addition, nonstructural 
safety 
measures 
were 
recommended 
and 
would 
be 
taken 
by 
the design 
engineers, 
such 
as 
installation 
of 
instrumentation 
and 
monitoring 
devices, 
O&M 
plans, 
including 
emergency  preparedness 
plans, 
and 
establishment 
of 
an 
independent 
panel 
of 
experts 
who 
would 
supervise 
final 
designs 
and 
safety 
measures. 
To 
avoid 
any 
landscape 
disfiguration,  construction 
contract  documents 
would 
stipulate that 
stone and 
gravel 
would 
only 
be 
provided 
by 
official 
quarries. 
The  Summary 
of 
the 
Dam 
Safety 
Report 
is 
part 
of 
the 
EM. 
Together 
with 
the 
full 
Dam 
Safety Report, 
it 
is 
also 
available 
in 
the 
Project File. 
Land 
Acauisition 
No 
involuntary 
population 
resettlement 
would 
be required 
in 
the 
identified 
dam 
sites. 
In 
the 
majority 
of 
cases, 
the 
land 
to 
be 
flooded 
would 
be 
“common  land”, 
not 
used 
for 
productive 
purposes 
and 
owned 
by 
the community. 
Nevertheless, there 
may 
be 
cases 
when individually-owned 
land may 
be 
inundated, 
in 
which 
case 
GDI’s 
pragmatic, 
demand-based 
approach 
for 
involuntary 
‘land 
acquisition’ 
would 
apply. 
GDI 
traditionally 
responds 
to 
a 
community 
demand 
for 
a 
dam 
by 
carrying 
out 
a survey 
of 
the 
area, 
identifying 
the 
land 
that 
would 
be 
flooded. 
The 
communities 
and 
responsible 
LCs 
internally 
discuss 
and 
agree 
on 
a compensation 
(in 
most 
cases 
a substitute 
land 
area) 
and 
sign 
a 
tripartite 
contract (community, 
LC, 
MAI-GDI) 
that 
no 
compensation 
from 
GOY 
is 
required. 
The 
land 
contribution 
constitutes 
the 
community’s 
share 
towards 
the 
construction 
of 
the 
dam 
and 
the 
benefits 
derived 
from 
it. 
If 
no 
agreement 
is 
reached, 
no 
darn 
is 
built. 
This 
policy 
has 
been 
in 
force 
since 
1994 and 
has 
so 
far 
never 
led to 
a 
dispute 
(Letter 
from 
MAI 
on 
Land 
Acquisition 
PoZicy 
- 
March 
11, 
2002 
- 
Project 
File). 
However, 
to 
cover 
the 
possibility 
that 
some 
involuntary 
expropriation 
might 
be 
necessary, 
the 
policy 
on 
Involuntary 
Resettlement 
has 
been declared 
applicable 
and 
actions 
to 
ensure 
compliance 
are 
presented 
in 
Section 
7.2 
- 
30 
- 
Page  35
below. 
Pest 
Management 
Pesticides 
and fertilizers 
are 
used 
freely 
and increasingly. 
Even 
though 
there 
is 
no 
direct linkage 
between 
the 
rising 
use 
and 
the 
proposed 
project, 
the 
EL4 
proposes a 
model 
PMP 
to 
be applied 
in 
the 
project 
areas 
for 
grapes, 
the 
second 
most  prevalent 
crop 
(first 
is 
qat). 
During 
the 
first 
half 
year 
of 
project 
implementation, 
a 
similar 
PMP would 
also 
be 
designed 
for 
qat. 
These 
two 
plans 
would 
cover 
about 
70 
percent 
of 
all 
crops planted 
in 
the basin. 
Other 
plans, 
notably 
for 
vegetables, 
would 
also 
be 
designed. 
These 
plans  are 
at 
once  a 
mitigation 
measure 
against 
harm 
that 
may 
occur 
from 
excessive use 
of 
chemicals, a 
protection 
of 
public 
health 
and 
a 
learning 
instrument 
for 
DPP 
within 
MAI, 
which 
would 
be 
in 
charge 
of 
implementing 
it, 
DPP 
would 
gain 
experience 
with 
alternative 
means 
to 
combat 
plant 
pests. 
If 
successful, 
these 
models 
could 
be 
replicated 
for 
other 
areas 
in 
the 
basin and 
the 
country. 
The 
sample 
PMP 
for 
grapes 
is 
part 
of 
the 
EIA 
and 
will 
also 
be 
included 
in 
the 
PIM 
and 
in 
the 
Project File. 
Other 
Potential 
Risks 
(i) 
Public 
health: 
Mitigation 
of 
any 
potential 
public 
health 
risks 
related 
to 
the 
project 
will 
be carried 
out 
by 
the 
MOPHP 
as 
part 
of 
its 
Bilharzia control 
program. 
Regular 
inspections 
of 
water bodies close 
to 
human  habitations 
will 
take 
place 
to 
detect 
and 
identify 
the  breeding 
of 
vectors, 
such 
as 
schistosomiasisbilharzia 
which 
is 
prevalent 
in 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin. 
Any 
aquatic 
snail 
colonies 
found 
will 
be 
eradicated 
through 
controlled 
spraying 
of 
molluscicides. 
Bilharzia 
statistics 
will 
be 
monitored 
with 
close 
attention 
to 
the 
project intervention 
sub-basins 
for 
any 
unusual 
pattern. 
Monitoring 
will 
be 
on 
a 
bi-annual 
basis 
as 
part 
of 
the 
ongoing 
effort 
by 
the 
existing clinics 
and health 
facilities 
in 
the 
project 
area. 
A 
public 
health 
bilharzia 
specialist 
will 
be 
available 
as 
a consultant, 
as 
part 
of 
the 
environmental 
resource 
team 
in 
the 
TS-SBC, 
in 
charge 
of 
developing 
and 
overseeing 
both 
health 
education 
and 
control 
measures. 
(ii) 
Shallow 
aauifer  water 
auality: 
A 
survey 
of 
water 
quality 
carried out 
in 
199511996 
by 
SAWAS 
sampled 
water 
in 
77 
hand-dug 
wells 
between 
20 
to 
100 
meters 
deep 
and 248 
boreholes 
between 
100 
and 
400 
meters 
deep 
in 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin. 
Tests 
indicate 
that generally the water 
quality 
is 
suitable 
for 
irrigation. 
As 
would 
be 
expected, 
the 
quality 
of 
the 
water 
in 
the 
shallow 
wells 
was 
poorer 
than 
the 
deeper 
boreholes. 
Biological 
testing 
and 
tests 
for 
pesticides 
residuals 
were 
not 
carried 
out, 
however 
in 
a 
number 
of 
boreholes 
and, 
in 
particular, 
in 
shallow 
wells 
the 
levels 
of 
Chlorides 
(CI), 
Sulfates 
(S04) 
and 
Nitrogen 
Trioxides 
(N03) 
exceed 
the 
WHO 
standards 
for 
drinking 
water. (Recommended 
limits for 
C1 
are 250mgl1, 
for 
SO4 
are 250mg/1 
-and 
for 
NO3 
SOmgll). 
One-third 
of 
shallow 
wells 
exceeded the 
limits 
for 
Chlorides, whereas 
all 
of 
the 
boreholes 
were 
below 
the 
limit. 
Additional 
water 
quality 
tests 
of 
samples 
of 
shallow 
wells 
in 
the 
project 
area 
were 
carried 
out 
during 
the 
project 
preparation. 
The 
presence 
of 
low 
levels 
of 
coliform 
bacteria 
found 
in 
the 
samples 
(9 
- 
16 
per 
100 
ml) 
indicates 
a 
degree 
of 
pollution 
that 
is 
not 
unusual 
in 
open 
wells 
in 
an agricultural 
area, 
where 
pollution 
is 
common 
from 
surface 
sources 
of 
animal and 
human 
wastes. 
Although, 
the 
quality 
of 
water 
for 
drinking 
is 
questionable 
without 
disinfection, 
it 
is 
highly 
improbable that 
any 
transfer 
of 
biological 
pollution 
to 
the 
lower 
aquifer 
could 
take place. 
This 
is 
confirmed 
by 
recent 
isotope 
tracing 
that 
indicates 
that 
the 
recharge 
rate 
of 
the 
lower 
fossil 
aquifer 
takes 
several 
decades 
during 
which 
pathogens 
could 
not 
survive.

on

one 
hand

through

irrigation 
and  aquifer

recharge,

risks

Aspects: 
Delegation 
of 
water management remulatow and enforcement 
responsibilities 
to 
local 
institutions: 
NWRA

The project team 
believes that

within 
the 
framework 
of 
a clear 
regulatory 
system 
(bylaws) 
by 
local 
communities, 
may 
be 
the most 
promising 
solution 
to 
come 
to 
grips 
with 
the current 
indiscriminate exploitation 
of 
the 
basin's 
water 
resources. 
Mitigating 
measures 
are: 
(a)

which

TA 
through 
the project; 
and 
(b) 
the 
introduction 
of 
bylaws 
that 
would 
foster 
delegation 
of 
these 
responsibilities (drafting 
of 
bylaws 
supported 
by 
TA). 
Nevertheless,  the  process 
will 
be 
slow 
and 
an 
endeavor 
for 
learning 
by 
experience. 
The 
Droiect 
mav 
be 
construed 
as 
assisting qat 
productiodexpansion:

consumption

(see 
A-1, 
above). 
But

It

Annex 
2: 
Detailed 
Project 
Description 
REPUBLIC 
OF 
YEMEN: 
Sana’a 
Basin Water Management Project 
SBWM 
project 
activities 
would 
be carried out 
in 
four 
representative sub-basin 
zones: 
in 
the 
basin’s 
Western Area 
(southern 
Hamdan 
and 
Bani 
Matar 
districts); 
South Eastem 
Area (Bani 
Bahloul, 
eastem 
part 
of 
Sanhan 
districts); 
Eastern 
Area (Bani 
Hushaish, 
northern 
Bani 
Al 
Harith, 
part 
of 
Khawlen 
districts); 
and 
North 
Eastern 
Area 
(Nihm, 
and 
part 
of 
Arhab 
districts) 
- 
see 
map 
for 
details. 
These 
areas 
were 
selected 
on 
the basis 
of: 
(a) 
representativeness 
of 
the 
sub-basins; 
(b) 
high 
chance 
of 
success 
in 
meeting project 
goals; 
and 
(c) 
favorable  environmental 
impact 
on 
the 
selected 
sub-basins, 
i.e.: 
- 
positive impact 
on 
the 
potential 
recharge; 
- 
scope 
for 
supply 
structures 
(dams) 
development 
and 
improvement; 
- 
scope 
for 
irrigation 
improvement and 
reduction 
in 
groundwater 
abstraction; and 
- 
favorable 
socio-economic 
aspects, 
communities’ 
willingness 
to 
participate. 
Project 
execution 
is 
planned 
to 
occur over 
a 
five-year 
project 
period. 
By 
Component; 
Project 
Component 
1 
- 
USSI1.70 
million 
Demand 
ManaPement 
and 
IrriPation 
Imnrovement 
This 
first 
component 
seeks 
to 
achieve 
water 
savings 
and 
conservation 
through 
interventions 
aimed 
primarily 
at 
increasing 
the 
efficiency 
of 
water 
use 
from 
irrigation 
systems 
in 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin. 
These 
are 
viewed 
as 
pilot 
interventions 
with 
a 
view 
to 
expansion 
in 
subsequent 
program 
phases. 
All 
of 
these 
interventions 
are 
planned 
to 
occur 
within 
the 
selected 
Phase 
I 
project 
area, 
which 
comprises 
all 
or 
part 
of 
10 
out 
of 
the 
22 
sub-basins 
of 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin. 
More 
precisely, 
the 
target 
irrigation 
improvement 
areas 
fall 
within 
8 
defined 
sub-catchments 
which 
have 
a 
total 
area 
of 
about 
127,300 
ha 
and 
a 
total 
irrigated 
area 
of 
about 
12,500 
ha; 
these 
areas 
are 
about 
40% 
and 
53% 
respectively 
of 
the 
corresponding 
total 
Sana’a 
Basin 
areas. 
The 
actual 
overall 
target 
irrigation 
improvement 
area 
is 
about 
4,000 
ha; 
this 
corresponds 
to 
3 
1 
% 
of 
the 
irrigated 
area 
within 
the 
8 
sub-catchments, 
and 
to 
17% 
of 
the 
irrigated 
area 
within 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin 
as 
a whole. 
The 
planned interventions 
largely 
comprise 
the 
improvement 
of 
delivery 
(conveyance 
and 
distribution) 
systems 
through 
upgrades 
of, 
or 
conversions to, 
piped 
systems, 
and 
the 
introduction 
of 
various 
modern 
on-farm 
pressurized 
irrigation 
technologies 
that 
have 
previously 
been 
proved 
effective 
in 
enhancing 
water 
use 
efficiencies.  The 
selected 
technology 
types 
and 
target 
areas 
for 
each 
of 
these 
have been 
arrived 
at 
on 
the basis 
of 
assessments 
of 
both 
technical 
suitability 
and 
user 
acceptability. 
They 
are 
summarized 
by 
sub-catchment 
in 
the 
table 
below. 
Further 
projected 
physical 
interventions 
include: 
(a) 
wadi 
bank 
protection 
works, 
for 
some 
vulnerable parts 
of 
the 
target 
irrigation 
area; 
(b) 
land levelling 
over 
3 
15 
ha, 
within 
the 
areas 
targeted 
for 
delivery 
system 
upgrades 
or 
conversions 
only; and 
(c) 
introduction 
of 
plastic 
tunneldcovers 
for 
10 
ha 
of 
demonstration 
plots, 
within 
the 
areas 
targeted 
for 
modem 
on-farm 
pressurized 
system 
installations.

Implementation 
of 
this 
component, 
including 
final 
assessment 
and 
selection 
of 
specific 
locations 
for 
each 
intervention 
type 
within 
each 
sub-catchment, 
is 
to 
be carried out 
with 
full 
community 
participation 
and 
agreement. 
Physical 
interventions 
are 
to 
be 
preceded 
by 
initial 
social 
mobilization 
efforts 
at 
village 
and 
farmer 
levels, 
and 
consequent 
establishment 
of 
suitable 
institutional 
arrangements 
for 
community-based 
water 
management, 
including 
WAS. 
They 
would 
then 
be carried 
out 
in 
parallel 
with 
capacity 
building 
and 
training 
programs. 
Users 
would 
thereby 
assume 
responsibilities 
for 
sustainable 
systems 
development, 
management, 
O&M, 
suitably 
supported 
by 
adequate 
extension 
services. 
Capital 
cost 
sharing 
principles 
proposed 
for 
this 
project 
component 
are 
based 
on 
those 
for 
the 
previous 
LWCP 
and 
its 
proposed 
successor, 
the Groundwater and 
Soil 
Conservation 
Project 
(GSCP). 
However, 
they 
have been 
modified 
to 
reflect 
the 
key 
objectives 
of 
the 
SBWMP. 
Specifically, 
increased 
levels 
of 
project 
subsidies 
to 
participating 
water 
users 
would 
serve 
as 
incentives 
to 
farmers 
to 
refrain 
from 
fiture 
use 
of 
the saved 
water 
and 
fiture 
extension 
of 
irrigated 
areas. 
Results 
of 
the special 
study 
carried 
out 
during 
project 
preparation 
are 
available 
in 
the 
project  file. 
The 
selected 
levels 
of 
beneficiary 
contributions 
to 
capital 
costs 
are 
indicated 
in 
the 
sections 
below 
for 
each 
type 
of 
proposed 
project 
intervention. 
It 
is 
intended 
that 
all 
O&M 
costs 
would 
be borne 
by 
the 
direct 
beneficiaries. 
Seven 
sub-components 
of 
this 
first 
project 
component have 
been 
defined. 
They 
are summarized 
below. 
Sub-component 
1 
(a) 
- 
Upgrading 
of 
piped-irrigation deliverv 
systems. 
This 
sub-component 
is 
directed at 
those 
irrigation 
areas 
that 
are 
already 
filly 
or 
partially 
equipped 
with 
GI 
or 
PVC 
pipeline 
conveyance 
and 
distribution 
systems, 
but 
that 
operate 
with 
lower-than-expected 
water 
use 
efficiencies 
due 
to 
the water 
delivery 
systems 
being 
either 
in 
disrepair 
or 
incomplete, 
The 
systems 
would 
be 
upgraded under 
the 
project 
to filly-piped 
closed 
systems 
delivery 
to 
field 
level, 
leading 
to 
estimated 
irrigation efficiency 
increases 
averaging 
about 
15%. 
The 
sub-component includes: 
(a) 
detail 
study, 
design  and 
supervision 
of 
system  improvements; 
(b) 
supply 
and 
installation 
of 
pipes 
and 
accessories; 
and 
(c) 
corresponding incremental 
O&M 
during 
the 
project 
period. 
The 
estimated 
average 
unit 
capital 
cost 
is 
US$8251ha 
and 
the 
target 
area 
is 
1,660 
ha, 
giving 
a 
total 
estimated 
capital 
cost 
of 
- 
44 
- 
Page  49
US$1,369,500. 
Adding 
the 
incremental 
O&M 
allowances 
gives 
an 
overall 
estimated 
baseline 
cost 
of 
US$l74S3,10O. 
The 
proposed 
levels 
of 
contribution 
to 
capital 
costs 
by 
direct 
beneficiary 
water 
users 
in 
this 
case 
are 
40% 
of 
inaterial 
costs 
and 
20 
% 
of 
installation 
costs. 
This  intervention 
type 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
non-controversial, 
relatively 
easy 
to 
implement, 
not 
very 
disruptive 
during 
implementation, 
and 
well 
supported 
by 
farmers. 
It 
is 
projected 
that, 
following initial 
satisfactory set-up 
of 
institutional 
arrangements, 
there 
would 
be quite 
intensive 
implementation activities 
for 
this 
sub-component 
from 
mid-Year 2 
to 
mid-Year 
4 
of 
the 
project 
period, 
with 
a 
subsequent 
tailing 
off 
of 
activities over 
the 
remaining 
period. 
Sub-comDonent 
1 
(b) 
- 
Conversion 
of 
open 
to 
piped-irrigation 
deliverv 
systems. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
this 
sub-component, 
the 
targets are 
irrigation 
systems 
that 
currently 
comprise 
conventional 
open channel 
networks 
and 
hence experience 
large 
water 
losses. 
Delivery 
systems 
would 
here 
be 
totally 
converted 
to 
fidly-piped 
closed 
systems 
down 
to field 
level. 
The 
irrigation 
efficiency 
increases 
that 
would 
result 
from 
this 
intervention 
type 
are 
estimated 
at 
about 
25%. 
Included 
in 
the sub-component 
are: 
(a) 
detailed 
study, 
design 
and 
supervision 
of 
system conversions; 
(b) 
supply 
and installation 
of 
pipes and 
accessories, 
PVC 
in 
some areas 
and 
GI 
in 
other 
areas; 
and 
(c) 
corresponding incremental 
O&M 
during 
the 
project 
period. 
Buried 
PVC 
pipelines 
are 
preferred and 
are 
to 
be 
used 
wherever  possible, 
but 
surface-laid 
GI 
pipelines 
are 
often 
needed 
where 
terrain 
is 
hilly 
and 
rocky. 
Estimated 
unit 
capital 
costs 
and 
target 
areas 
are 
US$1,02Sha 
and 
180 
ha 
for 
PVC 
pipeline 
systems, 
and 
US$1,2SOha 
and 
420 
ha 
for 
GI 
pipeline 
systems. 
The 
total 
target 
area 
is 
600 
ha, 
and 
the 
total 
estimated 
capital 
cost 
is 
US$709,500. 
Adding 
the 
incremental 
O&M 
allowances gives 
an overall 
estimated 
baseline cost 
of 
US$747,600. 
In 
this 
case 
also, 
the 
proposed 
capital 
cost 
contributions 
by 
the 
direct beneficiary 
water 
users are 
40% 
of 
material 
and 
20% 
of 
installation 
costs. 
Implementation 
aspects 
of 
this 
sub-component have been 
considered 
comparable 
to 
those 
of 
the 
previous 
one 
above, 
farmer 
acceptance 
included.  However, 
the 
preparatory study and design requirements 
would 
be 
greater, 
as 
would 
be 
the 
potential 
for 
disruption 
to 
irrigation 
operations. 
Following 
the 
initial 
institutional 
development 
efforts, 
quite 
intensive 
sub-component 
implementation activities 
are 
planned 
for 
the 
period 
from 
mid-Year 
2 
to 
mid-Year 
4, 
with 
reduced 
activities 
later. 
Sub-component 
1 
(c) 
- 
Introduction 
of 
modern 
on-farm 
pressurized 
irrigation 
systems. 
The 
two 
previous 
sub-components 
address 
the 
problem 
of 
water 
wastages 
due 
to 
deficiencies 
in 
the 
irrigation delivery 
(conveyance 
and 
distribution) 
systems 
down 
to 
but 
excluding 
the 
field 
level. 
Under 
this 
third 
sub-component, 
the 
introduction 
of 
various 
proven modern 
pressurized 
systems 
that integrate 
both 
distribution 
and 
field 
application 
systems at 
on-farm 
level 
is 
projected. 
This 
would 
be carried 
out 
in 
areas 
with 
conveyance 
systems 
that 
are 
already 
fully-closed 
pipeline 
systems. 
The resulting 
irrigation 
efficiency 
increases 
are 
estimated 
at 
about 
35%. 
The 
sub-component 
includes: 
(a) 
detailed 
study, 
design 
and supervision 
of 
system 
installations; 
(b) 
provision 
of 
head 
reservoirs; 
(c)

Quatemarly 
Alluvial 
aquifer, 
to 
allow 
farmers 
to 
pump 
from 
shallow

instead 
of 
deep 
aquifers 
that 
are 
critical 
for

supply 
for 
Sana'a 
City; 
(b) 
to 
rehabilitate 
existing 
darns 
that 
need 
urgent

to 
prevent 
potential dam 
failure 
as 
well 
as 
to

capacity

and

to

a sustainable

management 
system 
through 
strengthened 
capacity 
of 
MAI's 
GDI 
as 
well 
as 
participation 
of 
newly

WAS 
and 
WUFs 
in 
O&M 
activities 
and 
their 
fbding. 
During

after 
identifying 
and 
examining 
24 
existing 
dams 
requiring rehabilitation 
and 
9

and selection

carried 
out. 
Low 
safety 
hazard 
and

support 
led 
to initial 
elimination 
of 
a 
number 
of 
candidate 
dams. 
Ranking 
of

for

was 
then 
based 
primarily 
on 
recharge 
potential, 
safety 
hazard and 
social 
support 
factors.  Recharge 
potential 
and socio-environmental factors were 
the 
primaxy 
criteria 
used 
in 
the 
ranking 
of 
potential 
new 
darns. 
The 
process 
led 
to 
selection 
of 
11 
existing 
and 
5 
new 
dams 
to 
be 
covered under 
the project. 
Of

would 
be 
conventional 
dams 
while 
the

In 
relation 
to 
the 
selected 
11 
existing 
and 
4 
new 
conventional 
dams, 
the Component 2 
project provisions 
include 
for  final 
detailed 
studies  and 
designs, 
physical  works 
execution  and 
supervision, 
and 
corresponding incremental 
O&M 
within 
the 
period 
of 
the project. 
The 
component also 
provides 
for 
detailed 
definition 
studies 
relating to 
potential 
subsurface 
dams 
and other 
viable 
and 
cost-effective 
recharge 
systems, 
including 
a 
series 
of 
cascadelcheck 
dams, 
as 
well 
as 
an 
allowance 
for 
subsequent 
implementation 
of 
the 
most 
favored 
of 
these 
structures. 
Implementation 
of 
this 
component, 
including 
the 
final 
detailed 
studies 
and 
designs 
of 
dams, 
is 
to 
be 
carried out 
with 
the 
participation 
and 
full 
agreement 
of 
the 
affected 
communities 
and, 
above all, 
in 
consultation 
with 
those 
who 
are 
or would 
be 
entrusted 
with 
care 
and 
maintenance 
of 
the structures. 
In 
advance 
of 
physical 
interventions, 
social 
mobilization 
and 
financial 
assessment 
would 
take 
place at 
village 
and 
wadi 
branch

to 
set 
up 
suitable 
institutional 
arrangements 
for 
O&M, 
in 
the 
form 
of 
user

Works 
would 
then be carried 
out 
in 
parallel 
with 
capacity 
building 
and 
training 
programs 
for both 
dam 
management 
and extension 
service entities. 
For 
capital 
costs 
of 
works 
of 
this 
project 
component, 
it 
is 
not 
proposed 
to 
seek 
contributions 
from 
the 
beneficiary 
communities. 
This 
applies also 
to 
those 
O&M 
costs 
that relate 
directly 
to 
dam 
structure 
performance 
and 
safety. 
It 
is 
intended that 
GDI 
would 
take 
on 
the 
responsibility 
for 
such 
routine 
inspections 
and 
remedial 
works. 
However, 
it 
is 
proposed that the 
local 
beneficiary 
communities 
would 
carry out 
or 
arrange 
for: 
(a) 
dry 
season 
sediment 
removal 
from 
reservoirs 
to 
improve 
recharge; 
and 
(b) 
upkeep 
of 
dam 
and 
reservoir 
access roads, 
all 
on 
an 
annual 
basis. 
Beneficiary contributions 
to 
O&M 
costs 
may 
be 
between 
10% 
and 
20%, 
but 
will 
be 
assessed 
early 
on 
by 
sociallwillingness-to-pay 
surveys. 
Benefits 
of 
this 
component 
may 
accrue 
not only 
to 
the 
local 
sub-basin 
communities 
but 
also 
to 
water 
users 
in 
other

of 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin, 
During

of 
the

is 
expected 
that 
a 
better 
identification 
of

overall

would 
be
