Investigations 
and 
Assessment: 
This 
investigation 
and 
assessment 
package 
would 
aim 
at 
quantifying 
the  storage 
volume 
of 
the  Cretaceous 
Sandstone 
and 
overlying 
Quaternary 
Alluvial 
aquifers, 
both 
the 
portions 
already 
drained and the 
portions 
that 
would 
be drained over the 
coming 
years. 
It 
would 
focus 
on 
the 
aquifers 
underlying 
the 
irrigation 
areas 
in 
the 
vicinity 
of 
the 
NWSA 
NW 
and 
NE 
wellfields. 
The 
package 
would 
include: 
(a) 
construction 
of 
maps 
and 
sections 
to 
delineate 
the 
aquifers and the 
amount 
of 
historical 
depletion; 
(b) 
qualitative geological 
assessment 
of 
water 
storage properties; 
(c) 
drilling 
of 
three 
exploratory 
boreholes 
for 
core 
sampling, 
geophysical 
logging, groundwater 
sampling; 
and 
(d) 
laboratory 
testing 
of 
rock 
drill 
cores 
and 
field 
samples. 
The 
assessment 
would 
make 
use 
of 
both 
historic 
and 
current 
well 
inventory 
data 
relating 
to 
groundwater 
levels  and 
abstractions, 
and 
all 
available  geological 
information. 
Expected 
outputs 
include 
substantially 
improved 
estimates 
of 
hydrogeological 
characteristics and 
of 
groundwater 
availability. 
Three observation 
wells 
for 
future 
groundwater 
monitoring 
would 
also 
be 
drilled. 
Further details 
are 
available 
in 
the project file. 
The 
assessment 
would 
be 
carried out 
in 
the 
first 
year 
of 
the 
project 
by 
a 
small 
team 
of 
international 
and 
local 
consultants, 
and 
NWRA 
staff. 
The 
corresponding 
estimated baseline cost 
is 
US$804,400. 
(ii) 
Hvdrological 
Monitoring 
and 
Analvses: 
The  project 
would 
also 
provide 
for 
an  intensive 
hydrological  monitoring 
and 
analysis 
program 
at 
three 
levels: 
(a) 
sample  sub-basin 
level, 
for 
monitoring 
and analyzing 
of 
groundwater levels, 
abstraction 
volumes, chemistry, 
as 
well 
as 
rainfall 
and 
surface 
water  flows, 
to 
determine the 
impacts 
of 
recharge enhancement 
interventions 
and 
to 
establish 
sub-basin 
water 
balances; 
(b) 
field 
level, 
for 
monitoring 
and 
analyzing 
of 
water 
deliveries, 
beneficial 
ET, 
non-beneficial 
ET 
and evaporation, 
soil 
moisture 
content, 
runoff 
and 
deep 
percolation, 
to 
estimate 
field 
water 
balances 
and net water 
savinglrecharge  impacts; 
and 
(c) 
basin 
level, 
for 
deriving overall 
Sana’a 
Basin 
water 
balances. 
Further 
details 
are 
available 
in 
the 
project 
file. 
The 
basin-level 
hydrological monitoring 
would 
be 
conducted 
by 
the 
NWRA 
Sana’a 
Branch, 
while 
the sub-basin 
and 
field-level 
monitoring 
would 
be carried 
out 
by 
the 
specialized 
MAI 
monitoring 
and 
extension 
units 
(e.g. 
CWMU 
and 
AREA). 
Support 
in 
the 
form 
of 
equipment 
and 
incremental 
staff 
costs 
would 
be 
provided 
under  the  project. 
The 
corresponding  estimated 
baseline  cost 
is 
US$467,000. 
Specific 
hydrological 
analyses 
and 
evaluations 
would 
be 
performed 
at 
three 
points 
in 
time 
during 
the 
course 
of 
the 
project. 
An 
early (Year 
1) 
study 
for 
baseline 
assessment 
would 
be followed 
by 
middle 
(Year 
3) 
and 
late (Year 
5) 
studies 
for 
mid-term 
and 
fmal 
impact 
assessments. 
The 
results 
would 
be 
compared 
and combined 
with 
monitoring 
program 
results 
to 
date, 
as 
well 
as 
available 
historical 
data, 
current 
well 
inventory 
data, 
supplementary 
study-specific 
investigations, 
outputs 
from 
the 
aquifer 
assessment 
study 
(see 
above), 
from 
the satellite 
imagery and aquifer 
modeling 
studies 
(see 
below). 
A 
small 
team 
of 
international 
and 
local 
consultants 
would 
analyze 
the 
data 
with 
NWRA 
and 
MAI 
staff. 
The 
estimated baseline cost 
for 
this 
is 
US$214,800. 
(iii) 
Satellite 
Imagerv 
Studies: 
Advanced remote sensinglsatellite imagery 
studies 
with 
extended 
ground 
truthing 
would 
be performed 
at 
the 
same 
three 
points 
in 
time 
as 
the 
hydrological 
analyses 
(early, 
middle 
and

project 
period) 
and 
would 
provide 
input 
to 
all 
levels 
of 
these 
(field, 
sub-basin 
and 
basin). 
The 
studies 
would 
seek 
to 
estimate 
cropped 
and 
irrigated 
areas, 
cropping 
patterns 
and 
evapo-transpiration levels, 
all 
of 
which 
are 
important 
for 
project 
impact 
and water

International 
and

consultants supported 
by 
NWRA 
and 
MAI 
staff 
would 
be 
in 
charge 
of 
the 
- 
57 
- 
Page  62
studies. 
The 
estimated baseline cost 
for 
these 
is 
US$263,000. 
(iv) 
Aauifer  Modeling 
Studies: 
Based 
on 
the 
results 
of 
the 
hydrogeological 
investigations 
and 
assessments, 
refined 
aquifer 
characteristic  values 
and 
abstraction 
estimates 
would 
be 
used 
in 
the 
updating 
of 
the 
basin-wide aquifer 
model 
(USGS 
MODFLOW) 
and 
subsequent 
simulating 
of 
current 
(for 
verification) 
and 
potential 
future 
aquifer 
use scenarios. 
International and 
local 
consultants 
supported 
by 
NWRA 
and 
MAI 
staff 
would 
conduct the 
modeling 
work. 
The 
estimated 
baseline 
cost 
for 
this 
is 
US$59,400. 
(v) 
Monitoring 
Water 
Ouantitv. 
Oualitv 
and Pollution: 
A 
system 
would 
be 
developed 
for 
the 
selected 
four 
sub-basins 
to  monitor 
and 
record rainfall, 
wadi 
flow, 
evaporation, 
groundwater  level, 
and 
recharge 
rates. 
Data 
recording, 
collection 
and 
analysis 
mechanisms 
would 
be 
established, 
emphasizing 
sustainable 
community 
management. 
In 
particular, groundwater levels 
would 
be 
closely 
monitored 
to 
check 
pumping 
regulation 
and 
recharge effects. 
Deep boreholes 
and 
Sana'a 
basin-wide 
monitoring 
would 
also 
be 
conducted 
to 
better 
understand 
hydrogeological 
and 
geological formations 
and aquifer 
balance. 
The 
equipment, 
investigation 
and 
recurrent 
costs 
for 
monitoring, the latter 
on 
a 
declining 
basis, 
as 
well 
as 
training 
would 
be 
provided 
by 
the 
project. 
NWRA 
Sana'a 
Branch 
and 
its 
designated 
community 
groups 
would 
assume 
primary 
responsibly 
for 
these 
activities. 
Other 
monitoring 
measures are 
planned 
in 
the project, 
including 
groundwater 
pollution 
and pesticide 
residuals, 
to 
be monitored 
by 
NWRA 
Sana'a 
Branch 
with 
MAI 
PPD. 
As 
existing 
data 
in 
these 
areas 
are 
limited, 
it 
would 
be 
necessary 
to 
establish 
a baseline 
from 
which 
any 
changes 
can 
be 
measured. 
In 
addition,  the 
quality 
of 
WWTP 
effluent 
and 
sludge 
would 
be 
monitored 
by 
the 
Sana'a 
Local 
Corporation. 
EPA 
would 
ensure 
compliance 
with 
the established 
standards. 
The 
estimated 
baseline 
cost 
for 
this 
is 
US$252,000.

CamDaim

among 
the

at

is

I. 
ENVIRONMENTAL 
MANAGEMENT 
PLAN 
Regulation 
and 
Enforcement 
Water 
Law 
and 
Sana’a 
NWRA 
Branch: 
The 
two 
cornerstones 
of 
the 
EMP 
are 
regulation 
and 
enforcement. 
The 
enactment 
of 
the 
Water 
Law 
and 
the 
rapid 
drafting 
and 
issuance 
of 
the 
Executive 
Regulations 
or 
bylaws, 
which 
specifically 
state 
how 
the 
law 
is 
to 
be 
applied, 
would 
provide 
the 
foundation 
for 
this. 
The 
newly 
established 
NWRA 
Sana’a 
Branch 
(supported 
by 
TS-SBC 
and 
EPA) 
would 
be 
the 
main 
agency 
in 
monitoring 
and 
enforcing 
the 
Water 
Law 
and 
would 
ensure 
that 
the 
main 
provisions 
of 
the 
Mitigation 
Plan 
can 
be 
implemented. 
This 
includes licensing, 
drilling 
of 
new 
wells, 
establishing 
maximum 
abstraction 
rates, 
maintaining 
a 
database 
of 
wells 
and 
boreholes 
in 
the basin, 
monitoring 
water 
quality, and 
overall monitoring 
of 
water 
levels 
and 
quality 
with 
the 
basin 
aquifer. 
Dam 
Safety, 
Siltation 
and 
Downstream 
Effects 
An 
important 
component 
of 
the 
mitigation plan 
is 
to 
ensure 
that 
all 
dams 
and water 
recharge structures 
remain 
safe 
and 
do 
not 
pose a 
threat 
to 
human 
life 
and property. 
Eleven 
dams 
in 
need 
of 
urgent 
repair 
would 
be rehabilitated 
and 
five 
new 
recharge structures 
would 
be 
constructed 
to 
best 
engineering 
practice, supervised 
by 
TS-SBC. 
The 
GDI 
and 
the 
agencies, 
to whom 
TS-SBC 
may 
delegate 
its 
powers, 
would 
undertake 
regular  inspections 
of 
all 
dam 
structures 
to 
ensure 
their 
integrity 
and 
report 
any 
potential 
failures 
to 
the responsible authorities. 
It 
would 
monitor 
speedy 
repairs 
and 
rectification 
of 
any 
faults. 
A 
best 
practice 
of 
dam 
construction manual 
would 
be 
developed 
by 
GDI 
for 
use 
by 
any 
agency 
involved 
in 
dam 
construction. 
A 
dam 
safety 
expert 
would certify 
the 
soundness 
of 
both 
the 
new 
and 
rehabilitated 
dams. 
The 
dam 
sites 
would 
be 
selected 
to 
ensure 
that 
maximum 
infiltration 
would 
take 
place, Agreements 
would 
be 
reached 
with 
downstream 
fanners 
regarding periodic 
discharges 
of 
water 
to 
ensure 
minimum 
impact 
on 
spate 
irrigation 
and other possible 
ecological 
effects 
assessed 
to 
be 
minimal. 
In 
addition, 
farmers, 
as 
part of 
the 
agreement 
to 
construct  a 
dam, 
would 
be 
required 
to 
undertake 
annual 
desilting. 
The 
silt 
which 
has 
a 
high 
nutrient 
value 
can 
be 
used 
as 
a 
soil 
conditioner, 
or 
disposed 
of 
on 
common 
unproductive 
land. 
The 
summary 
of 
the 
dam 
safety 
review 
report produced 
during 
project 
preparation 
is 
available 
in 
the 
project 
file. 
Public 
Health 
The 
mitigation 
of 
any 
public 
health 
risks, 
although 
limited, 
in 
particular 
the 
breeding 
of 
mosquitoes 
and 
aquatic 
snails, 
the 
potential 
vectors 
of 
malaria and 
bilharzia 
would 
be 
carried 
out 
by 
the 
MOPHP 
through 
it’s 
eradication and 
control 
groups 
who 
have expressed 
willingness 
to 
collaborate 
with 
the 
project. 
The 
project 
would 
support 
their 
operations 
through 
funding 
and 
institution 
building. 
The 
control 
measures 
would 
include 
the 
regular inspections 
of 
water 
bodies 
close 
to 
human populations 
to 
detect 
and 
identify 
the 
breeding 
of 
vectors, 
and 
the 
control 
of 
any 
populations 
found 
through 
controlled 
spraying 
of 
insecticides 
and 
molluscicides. 
They 
would 
also advise 
the relevant authorities 
to 
apply physical 
control 
measures 
such 
as 
cutting 
back 
water-side 
vegetation 
and 
lowering 
the water 
level 
in 
the 
dams. 
These 
- 
59 
- 
Page  64
physical 
measures 
are 
very 
effective 
in 
controlling 
the 
bilharzia 
vector. 
In 
parallel, 
as 
part 
of 
the 
communications 
and 
education 
component of the 
project, 
good 
sanitation 
and 
personal 
hygiene 
practices 
would 
be 
promoted 
in 
order 
to 
reduce the transmission 
of 
water-based 
disease 
vectors. 
Any 
increase 
in 
the incidence 
of 
malaria 
andfor 
bilharzia 
would 
be 
monitored through 
the 
monthly 
records 
maintained 
by 
clinics 
and 
health 
facilities 
in 
the 
project 
area. 
In 
order 
to 
reap 
the social 
benefits 
of 
the 
project, 
improve 
public 
health 
and 
in 
particular 
to 
mitigate 
against water-based 
disease 
vectors, 
it 
is 
essential 
to 
improve 
domestic 
water 
supplies 
in 
terms 
of 
quality 
and accessibility, 
and 
personal 
hygiene. 
A 
campaign 
to provide 
increased coverage 
of 
public 
water 
supply 
stand 
posts 
and 
latrines 
should 
be 
launched, together 
with 
a 
hygiene  education 
program 
that 
emphasizes 
safe excreta 
disposal 
and 
hand 
washing. 
Land 
Acquisition 
No 
involuntary 
resettlement 
is 
anticipated 
in 
the 
project.  However, 
agreements 
would 
have 
to 
be 
reached 
with 
the 
local 
community regarding 
land 
acquisition. 
The 
GDI, 
under 
the 
MAI, 
differentiates  between 
land 
acquisition 
for 
large 
public 
works  projects 
and the 
small 
dams 
that 
benefit 
discrete 
farming 
communities. 
Since 
1994, 
GDI 
has 
adopted a pragmatic, demand-based approach 
to 
the 
acquisition 
of 
land 
for 
dam 
construction when 
it 
is 
primarily 
for 
the 
benefit 
of 
the 
local 
community. 
The 
procedure 
is 
as 
follows: 
After 
GDI 
receive 
a request 
from 
a 
particular community 
to 
construct 
a 
dam, 
they respond 
by 
conducting 
a 
survey 
of 
the 
area 
and 
producing 
a design 
which 
defines 
the 
land 
requirement 
and 
land 
ownership. 
This 
is 
discussed 
with 
the 
community and 
if 
they 
agree 
to 
go 
ahead, 
they 
sign 
a 
contract 
with 
GDI 
to 
provide 
the 
land 
as 
a 
contribution 
to 
the 
costs. 
The 
communities 
themselves, 
through 
an 
internal 
process, agree 
on 
compensation 
for 
individuals 
whose 
land 
would 
be 
built 
over 
or 
inundated, 
most 
often 
by 
providing land 
in 
an 
alternative location. 
Similarly, 
if 
there 
are 
conflicts 
with 
downstream 
or 
adjacent 
communities, 
they 
must 
reach 
a documented 
agreement 
on 
allocation 
of 
water 
rights 
before 
GDI 
would 
commence 
construction 
of 
the 
dam. 
Similar 
practices 
are 
adopted 
by 
the 
Social 
Development 
Fund 
Project 
and 
the 
Agriculture 
and 
Fisheries 
Development 
Fund. 
This 
procedure 
complies 
with 
the Bank‘s 
Operational Policies, 
if 
the 
agreement 
is 
mutual 
and 
is 
registered 
as 
described 
above. 
The 
following 
translation 
from 
Arabic 
is 
of 
the 
form 
used 
by 
MA1 
to 
reach 
agreement 
with 
the 
communitylland 
owners 
regarding contributing 
land 
for 
dam 
construction. 
The 
form 
is 
signed 
and 
dated 
by 
representatives 
of 
MA1 
and 
the community. 
“We, 
the 
undersigned and owners 
of 
this 
land,  declare 
that 
our 
land 
can 
be 
used 
for 
the 
implementing 
of 
this 
project. 
An 
agreement has 
been reached 
with 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
and 
Irrigation 
declaring 
that 
we 
are 
ready 
to 
give 
up 
our 
land 
for 
the establishment 
of 
this 
project. 
This 
agreement 
includes 
giving 
up 
the 
lake, rocks, 
soil, 
and 
water 
and 
anything 
else 
located 
on 
the 
land 
that 
can 
be 
of 
use 
for 
project 
implementation. 
We 
also declare 
that 
we 
would 
take 
full 
responsibility 
of 
constructing 
a 
road 
through 
which 
cars 
trucks 
and other 
project 
requirements 
could utilize 
at 
our 
expense”. 
Therefore, 
it 
is 
important when 
surveying 
any 
potential 
dam 
site 
to 
determine  the 
extent, 
use 
and 
ownership 
of 
the 
land 
(if 
any) 
that 
would 
be 
required and 
to 
enter 
into 
a 
dialogue 
and 
agreement 
with 
the 
community 
regarding 
the 
transfer. 
This 
should be 
fully 
documented 
and 
legally 
registered 
as 
described 
above. 
Productive 
and private 
land 
should be avoided 
to 
the extent 
possible. 
An 
observer 
should 
ensure 
that 
an 
equitable process 
has 
taken 
place. 
This 
may 
entail 
short-term 
inputs 
from 
a 
social 
scientist. 
As 
in 
the 
majority 
of 
cases, 
this 
would 
be 
“common 
land”, 
the 
impact 
would 
be 
minimal. 
A 
copy 
of 
an 
official 
letter 
from 
MAI 
outlining 
its 
land 
acquisition 
policy 
is 
available 
in 
the 
project file.

Involuntary 
Resettlement 
(OPIBP 
4.12) 
In 
the 
event,  however,  that 
involuntary expropriation may be 
required, 
the 
Borrower 
has 
prepared 
a 
Resettlement 
Policy 
Framework 
(RPF) 
acceptable 
to 
IDA 
and 
disclosed 
it 
locally 
and 
through 
the 
Infoshop. 
The 
RPF 
is 
presented 
as 
Annex 
11. 
When 
a 
dam 
site 
is 
identified, 
the 
Borrower 
submits 
to 
IDA 
a 
Land 
Acquisition 
Plan 
identifying 
the 
site 
and 
its 
key 
characteristics, 
including 
ownership, 
evidence 
of 
consultations and 
i~ormed~ommunity 
agreement, 
and 
expected adverse socio-economic 
impacts, 
if 
any. 
IDA 
advises the 
Borrower 
whether 
a Resettlement 
Action 
Plan 
(RAP) 
is 
required 
for 
that 
site, 
and 
further 
action 
must 
be 
taken 
to 
ensure 
and 
demonstrate 
full 
community 
consensus, 
or 
whether 
approval 
is 
then 
dependent 
on 
IDA’S 
approval 
of 
the 
RAP. 
Wastewater 
Treatment, Wastewater 
Reuse 
and 
Sludge Reuse 
The 
operations 
and functioning 
of 
the 
WWTP 
should be 
modified 
and 
improved 
as 
a 
separate endeavor, 
so 
as 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
effluent 
does 
not 
pose a 
public 
health 
risk, 
nor 
pollute 
the 
aquifer. 
Mitigation 
requires 
major 
upgrading 
of 
the 
WWTP, 
including 
the 
construction 
of 
primary 
sedimentation 
tanks 
and 
a 
holding 
lagoon 
in 
which 
to 
store 
excess 
flows 
until 
it 
can 
be 
treated 
in 
the 
plant. 
Operatives 
in 
the 
WWTP 
and 
sludge reuse 
facilities should be 
provided 
with 
protective 
clothing 
and 
given 
adequate 
safety 
training 
by 
the 
Sana’a 
Local 
Corporation. 
The 
irrigation 
system 
should be 
improved 
by 
providing 
a 
balancing lagoon 
and 
improved 
distribution 
system. 
In 
addition,  a sludge 
drying 
and 
reuse system 
should 
be developed 
for 
reuse 
as 
a 
soil 
conditioner. 
The 
chemical and 
biological quality 
of 
wastewater 
and 
sludge 
that 
is 
to 
be 
reused 
should 
be regularly 
tested 
by 
the 
Sana’a 
Local 
Corporation 
to 
ensure 
that 
it 
does 
not 
pose a 
threat 
to 
public 
health. 
NWRA 
Sana’a 
Branch should 
also 
carry out 
spot checks 
to 
ensure 
compliance 
with 
WHO 
standards. 
A 
detailed 
study 
should be 
conducted 
to 
determine the 
best 
technical 
solutions 
and 
costs 
of 
these 
improvements. 
Implementation 
of 
the 
SBWM 
Project should be 
contingent 
on 
satisfactory 
parallel 
implementation 
of 
WWTP 
improvements. 
Pest 
Control 
and 
Pesticides 
Management 
The 
excessive 
and  improper 
use 
of 
pesticides 
and 
fertilizers 
would 
be 
mitigated 
through 
the 
demonstration 
of 
a 
model 
integrated 
pest 
management 
(IPM) 
plan 
in 
the 
project 
areas 
for 
the 
cultivation 
of 
grape. 
This 
would 
serve 
as 
a component 
of 
the 
farmer education 
initiative 
which would 
include 
“field 
days” 
and 
a 
mobile 
workshop 
which would 
stress 
good 
and 
safe 
practice 
for 
pesticides 
application 
and 
storage 
in 
the 
field. 
In 
addition, 
regular testing 
for 
pesticides residue 
would 
take place. 
These 
activities 
would 
be 
undertaken 
by 
the 
MAI 
DPP 
over a 
two-year period. 
Details 
of 
the 
IPM 
Plan 
are 
available 
in 
the 
project file. 
In 
addition, 
an 
IPM 
for 
qat 
would 
be 
prepared 
within 
six 
months 
of 
project 
effectiveness. 
Awareness 
and 
Education 
A 
number 
of 
awareness 
building, 
training 
and education 
initiatives 
are 
proposed 
in 
the 
project 
and 
described 
elsewhere. 
General 
messages 
and 
information 
regarding 
environmental 
mitigation 
measures are 
integrated 
within 
the communication 
strategy. 
Health 
and 
hygiene education 
is 
an 
important 
aspect 
of 
reducing 
the 
risks 
of 
malaria  and bilharzia. 
The 
costs 
are 
included 
in 
the 
awareness 
and 
education 
campaign 
budgets.

- 
Page  66
Short-Term 
Construction 
Risks 
The 
short-term 
environmental 
pollution 
risks 
associated 
with 
the construction 
phase 
of 
the 
project 
would 
be 
mitigated 
through 
a 
number 
of 
measures, 
most 
of 
which 
would 
be 
stipulated 
in 
the 
terms 
of 
contract 
with 
the 
building 
contractor. 
These 
would 
include 
control 
of 
oil 
and 
diesel 
spillages, 
control 
of 
quarrying, 
dust 
suppression, 
construction 
of 
access 
roads and 
reporting 
of 
“chance 
finds” 
of 
artifacts 
of 
archeological  importance. 
Compliance 
with 
these 
measures 
would 
be 
the 
responsibility 
of 
TS-SBC 
which 
would 
supervise the works. 
Non-compliance 
would 
be 
penalized 
through 
invocation 
of 
penalty 
clauses. 
During 
the 
construction 
of 
the works,  water 
quality 
safeguards 
should 
ensure 
that 
vehicle 
fueling 
and 
maintenance 
areas are 
bounded 
and 
located at 
least 
100 
m 
from 
watercourses, 
or 
recharge 
areas. 
The 
contractor(s) 
would 
progressively rehabilitate 
the 
work 
areas 
to 
ensure 
that 
disturbed 
sites, 
which 
are 
susceptible 
to 
erosion, 
would 
not 
remain 
exposed for 
long 
periods. 
To 
the extent 
possible, 
the 
work 
should 
not 
be 
carried 
out 
during 
the 
rainy 
season(s). 
The 
borrow 
pits 
or 
quarries 
would 
be carefully 
designated 
by 
the 
environmental 
specialist 
of 
TS-SBC 
to 
cause 
the  least 
possible 
impacts. 
The 
construction 
of 
temporary 
roads 
to 
reach the 
dam 
sites 
may 
cause 
disruption of 
some 
cultivated 
lands 
and 
terraces 
and 
must 
be carefully routed 
in 
consultation 
with 
the 
local 
community and 
then 
be 
regularly 
watered 
to 
suppress 
dust. 
Noise 
and 
dust 
would 
affect 
communities 
adjacent 
to 
construction 
sites, 
particularly when 
heavy 
equipment 
is 
in 
use 
and the contractor 
would 
be 
required 
to 
maintain 
it 
in 
good 
order 
and 
use 
silencing 
and 
dust 
suppression 
measures, 
when 
appropriate. 
The health 
and 
safety 
of 
the 
work 
force 
would 
be 
protected 
as 
part 
of 
the 
contractual 
arrangements. 
This 
would 
include the 
provision 
of 
safe 
water 
supply 
and 
appropriate 
sanitation at 
the campsite. Awareness 
raising 
and other 
safety 
measures 
would 
be 
introduced 
to 
eliminate 
or 
prevent the 
risks 
of 
injuries 
due 
to 
accidents. 
Public Consultation Mechanism 
As 
an 
extension 
of 
the 
public 
disclosure 
principle 
and 
practice throughout 
the 
project 
implementation 
process, 
a 
point 
of 
contact 
would 
be 
maintained 
in 
the 
field 
to 
which 
the 
public 
may 
refer 
for 
information 
or 
lodge any 
grievance 
or 
concerns. 
The 
public 
consultation 
mechanism 
would 
be 
maintained 
by 
the 
NWWSB 
or 
TS-SBC 
throughout the 
life 
of 
the project.

part

MPH 
Bilhazia 
and 
Malaria 
Eradication 
Units

monitoring 
and

for 
the

dosage 
rates

and

EPA 
in

regulation 
and 
enforcement 
responsibilities.

Contracts 
It 
is 
essential 
that 
environmental  conditions 
are 
included 
in 
any 
tender documents and 
conditions 
of 
contract 
for 
any 
construction  works. 
These 
would 
include 
instructions 
to 
the 
contractor  regarding 
environmental 
mitigation 
measures 
to 
be 
observed 
during 
construction 
and 
processes 
to 
be 
followed 
in 
case 
of 
any 
“chance 
fmds” 
of 
any 
artifacts 
or 
rare 
species 
of 
flora or 
forna 
are 
found. 
Environmental 
Resource 
Team 
An 
Environmental 
Specialist 
would 
be placed 
in 
the 
TS-SBC 
to 
monitor 
the 
EMP. 
Plus 
a 
local 
resource 
team 
would 
be 
identified 
that can 
be called 
upon 
from 
time 
to 
time 
in 
the event 
that any artifacts 
or 
rare 
species 
of 
flora or 
forna 
are 
found. 
The 
team 
would 
consist 
of 
a 
biodiversity 
and 
heritage 
specialist 
who 
could 
then 
readily 
make 
assessments 
regarding 
the 
significance 
of 
any 
such 
findings. 
Coordination 
of 
Communications 
A 
number 
of 
communication 
interventions 
are 
planned 
within 
the  project, 
which 
include 
public 
awareness, 
farmer education, pesticides management 
and 
health 
and 
hygiene 
education. 
These 
should 
be 
well 
coordinated 
so 
as 
to 
ensure 
consistent 
messages 
and 
in 
order 
to 
share 
resources. 
In 
addition, 
these 
should 
be 
integrated, whenever possible, 
into 
the 
existing 
extension 
services 
and 
designed 
so 
that 
they 
can 
be institutionalized 
after the projects 
completion. 
Urban 
Water 
Supply Demand 
Management 
The project 
goes 
some 
way 
in 
addressing the 
water 
resource management issues 
in 
the 
rural 
areas 
of 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin 
which 
has 
an 
indirect 
effect 
on 
urban water 
supplies. 
It 
is 
recommended that 
a 
more 
proactive 
approach 
is 
taken 
toward 
demand management 
in 
the 
city 
of 
Sana’a. 
A 
number 
of 
urban 
demand management 
measures 
can 
be 
utilized 
through 
water 
pricing,  leak detection and 
repair. 
These 
measures, 
although 
beyond 
the 
scope 
of 
the 
proposed project, 
should 
be 
introduced 
in 
the 
parallel 
urban 
projects. 
In 
addition, 
provision 
should be 
made 
at 
the 
WWTP 
to 
dispose 
of 
septic 
tank 
wastes 
carried 
by 
vacuum 
tankers. 
Flexibility 
Given 
the 
limited 
knowledge 
of 
the complex water 
management 
situation 
in 
the 
basin 
in 
the 
initial 
stage, 
enough 
flexibility 
should be 
allowed 
in 
the 
early 
stages 
of 
project 
implementation 
to 
readjust the 
project 
strategies, 
scope 
and 
procedures 
as 
more 
knowledge 
is 
gained 
through 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation. 
Project 
Component 
6 
- 
US$1.50 
million 
Project 
Manapement 
and 
Monitoring 
As 
previously 
indicated, 
the 
favored project 
management 
approach 
maximizes implementation through 
contracts 
for 
execution 
of 
project 
tasks. 
This 
leads 
to 
formulation 
of 
a 
TS-SBC 
whose 
role 
is 
one 
of 
overall 
project 
oversight, 
direction,  coordination,  review, 
procurement, 
administration 
and 
monitoring 
rather 
than 
of direct implementation.  The 
result 
is 
a 
quite 
compact 
unit 
that 
would, 
however, 
need 
to 
be 
- 
65 
-

staffed 
by 
well-qualified 
and motivated 
professionals 
who 
are 
specialized 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
relevance 
to 
the 
project. 
For 
the 
management, 
direction, 
coordination 
and 
review 
functions,

required 
specialization 
areas 
are:

and

(b) 
engineering and 
water resource 
management; 
and 
(c)

and

For

procurement, 
administration 
and 
monitoring 
functions they 
are:(i) 
procurement 
and 
contract 
management; 
(ii) 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation; 
(iii) 
financial 
management 
and

and 
(iv) 
office 
administration. 
To

is 
made 
for

full-time 
TS-SBC

of

A

specialist 
would 
be 
TS-SBC's

A

of 
full-time local 
core

for

functions, 
driving 
and 
guard 
duties, 
is 
also 
provided 
for. 
An

to 
be

to 
coordinate 
and 
oversee 
the  project's 
environmental

activities 
within 
TS-SBC. 
Essential 
short-term 
TA 
to 
TS-SBC 
by 
wellqualified

is

of

consultants 
and

timely 
technical

monitoring 
and

processes 
and

imgation

1. 
Increasing 
the 
useful 
lfe 
of 
groundwater 
resources 
in 
the 
Sana 
'a 
Basin 
is 
the 
primary 
objective 
of 
the 
program, 
of 
which 
the 
proposed 
project 
is 
the 
first 
phase. 
Accordingly, 
the 
project 
will: 
(a) 
improve 
the 
knowledge 
of 
the 
water 
balance 
and 
usable groundwater 
storage; 
and 
(b) 
test a 
combination 
of 
technical, 
financial, 
regulatory 
and 
institutional 
instruments 
aimed 
at 
increasing the 
availability 
of 
water 
(supply 
management) 
and 
decreasing 
its 
demand (demand 
management). Subsequent phases 
of 
the 
program 
will 
be 
designed 
based 
on 
the 
findings 
of 
the 
first 
phase, 
allowing 
a 
better understanding 
of 
the 
resource 
and 
of 
the 
measures 
to 
be 
implemented 
to 
best reduce 
the 
rate 
of 
aquifer 
depletion. 
A 
strong 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
system 
will 
be 
set-up 
as 
part 
of 
project 
activities. 
32 
129 
Given 
current 
knowledge 
of 
the 
hydrogeology, 
it 
is 
not 
possible 
to 
quantify 
by 
how 
much 
the 
project 
would 
extend 
the useful 
life 
of 
groundwater  resources. Indeed, 
the 
groundwater 
imbalance 
and the 
exploitable 
groundwater 
storage 
are 
fraught 
with 
high 
degree 
of 
uncertainties; 
and 
the 
overall 
effect 
of 
project 
interventions 
on 
the 
aquifer 
system 
is 
to 
a 
large 
extent 
unknown. 
A 
more direct way 
of 
estimating 
project 
benefits 
was 
used consisting 
of 
aggregating, 
at the 
level 
of 
the 
basin, expected 
Net 
Water 
Savings 
(NWS) 
and estimating their 
economic value 
according 
to 
their 
expected 
future 
use. 
NWS 
are 
expected 
from 
a 
decrease 
in 
the 
non-beneficial 
use 
of 
water 
(i.e., 
evaporation) 
while 
limiting 
growth 
in 
beneficial 
use 
(i.e., 
water 
absorbed 
by 
the 
crops), 
In 
practice, 
it 
entails increasing 
the 
irrigation 
efficiency 
through 
the  dissemination 
of 
improved irrigation 
techniques 
and 
enhancing groundwater 
recharge 
from 
surface 
run-off 
through 
the 
construction 
and 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
dams, 
while 
preventing 
irrigation 
expansion. 
NWS 
have 
been 
quantified 
for two 
components, 
namely 
the 
irrigation 
improvement 
component 
(40% 
of 
project 
costs) 
and 
the 
groundwater 
recharge component 
(30% 
of 
project 
costs). 
NWS 
are 
expected 
to 
reach 
about 
8 
MCMfyear 
at 
full 
development 
or 
about 
5% 
of 
current 
annual 
consumptive 
use 
and 
6% 
of 
current 
annual 
groundwater 
mining 
in 
the 
Sana'a 
Basin. 
Assuming 
a constant 
mining 
of 
the 
aquifer over 
time 
(129 
MCWyear), 
the 
project 
would 
extend the 
useful 
life 
of 
the 
aquifer 
by 
5%.

(iii) 
Valuation 
of 
the 
water savings 
The methodology 
proposed 
consists 
of: 
(a) 
estimating 
the amounts 
of 
the 
NWS 
that 
would 
be 
used 
in 
the 
M&I 
and 
agricultural 
sectors 
respectively; 
and 
(b) 
measuring 
the value 
of 
water 
in 
these 
two 
sectors. 
Only 
the user 
value 
of 
water was calculated. 
The 
scarcity value that 
will 
increase 
over 
time 
has 
not 
been 
quantified. 
The 
base-case 
scenario 
assumes 
that 
the 
allocation 
of 
the 
NWS 
between 
the 
M&I 
and 
irrigation 
sectors 
follows 
the current 
and 
expected 
allocation of 
overall 
water 
resources 
between 
these 
two 
sectors 
in 
the 
basin. 
Two 
other 
scenarios 
were considered 
(see 
the 
sensitivity 
analysis), 
in 
which 
100% 
of 
the 
NWS 
is 
used 
in 
irrigation 
and 
100% 
is 
used 
in 
for 
M&I 
use. 
The actual 
allocation 
being 
somewhere 
between 
these 
two 
extremes. 
In 
the 
base-case 
scenario, 
the 
share 
of 
water allocated 
to 
M&I 
as 
well 
as 
the value 
of 
water 
increase 
over 
time. 
The 
current 
gross 
M&I 
water 
use 
is 
about  38 
MCMlyear 
(31 
MCM 
for 
municipal 
use 
in 
urban 
areas, 
3 
MCM 
for 
domestic 
use 
in 
rural 
areas 
and 
4 
MCM 
for 
industrial 
use) 
of 
which 
80% 
returns 
to 
the aquifer. 
Hence, 
consumptive 
use 
in 
M&I 
is 
about 
7 
MCMlyear. 
Current 
net 
water 
use 
in 
irrigation 
is 
estimated 
at 
about 
154 
MCM 
(with 
an 
average 
retum 
flow 
of 
20%). 
The 
irrigation 
sector accounts for 
96% 
of 
consumptive 
use 
in 
the 
basin, 
the 
M&I 
sector 
4%. 
It 
is 
assumed 
that 
water 
use 
in 
irrigation 
and 
domestic 
use 
in 
rural 
areas 
would 
remain 
constant 
over 
time. 
Industrial 
water 
use 
would 
increase 
by 
2% 
pea. 
between 
2003 
and 
2013, 
and 
1% 
p.a. 
between 
2013 
and 
2023. 
While municipal 
water 
use 
in 
the 
urban 
area 
will 
increase 
as 
a 
result 
of 
population growth 
(6% 
p,a, 
between 
2003-13 
and 
5% 
thereafter) 
and 
increase 
in 
the 
average  use 
per 
capita 
(2% 
p.a. 
between 
20 
13-2023 
and 
1 
% 
thereafter). 
The 
economic value 
of 
water 
in 
agriculture 
is 
estimated 
using 
the 
residual 
imputation 
method 
(the 
value 
added 
of 
water 
is 
calculated 
on 
the 
basis of 
farm 
budgets 
and 
average 
cropping 
pattern 
in 
the 
project 
area). 
The 
average 
cropping pattern 
has 
been estimated 
by 
Satellite 
Data 
Analysis 
(Sana’a 
Basin

and 
is

to 
remain 
constant 
over 
time. 
Qat 
is 
the 
main 
crop 
covering 
about 47% 
of 
the 
irrigated 
area, 
followed 
by 
grape 
(35%), 
mixed 
cereals 
and 
vegetables 
(16%) 
and 
fruit 
trees 
(2%). 
Qat 
is 
by 
far, the 
most profitable 
crop 
in 
the basin 
and 
cannot 
be 
excluded 
from 
the 
project.  Under 
current 
price 
structure,  qat 
is 
financially  viable 
even 
when the 
water  table 
is 
deeper 
than 
800 
meters, 
whereas 
vegetable 
and 
fruit 
trees 
becomes 
unviable 
at 
about 300-400 meters. 
Farm 
budgets have been 
prepared 
for 
these 
crops  based 
on 
a representative  sample 
of 
farms 
in 
the 
project 
area. 
It 
was  assumed 
that 
agricultural 
practices and 
input 
and output 
prices 
would 
remain 
constant, 
except 
for 
pumping 
costs. 
Pumping 
cost 
is 
increasing 
over 
time 
as 
the 
depth of 
the water  table 
increases 
by 
an 
average 
of 
4 
meterslyear. 
Detailed 
farm budgets 
and 
the 
calculation 
of 
economic 
prices 
are 
available 
in 
the 
project 
files. 
Based 
on 
these assumptions, 
the 
average 
economic value 
added 
of 
consumutive water used 
in

is 
estimated 
at 
0.26 
US$/& 
in 
2003 (assuming a 
20% 
retum 
flow).

economic 
value 
for 
municipal 
use 
was 
estimated 
using

method 
(based 
on

paid 
to 
consumers 
to 
private 
tankers 
(on 
average 
US1.2$/m3) 
as

WTP 
is 
available.

an

3. 
Agricultural 
benefits. 
In 
addition 
to 
saving 
water, 
irrigation 
improvement 
has a 
positive 
impact 
on 
farmer 
revenues 
as 
crop 
yields 
and 
produce 
quality 
improve, and 
labor 
and 
pumping 
costs 
decrease. 
At 
full 
development, 
yield 
increases are 
expected 
to 
range 
from 
2 
to 
4% 
and 
the 
reduction 
in 
the 
volume 
of 
water 
pumped 
from 
18 
to 
36% 
depending 
on 
the 
type 
of 
irrigation 
improvement 
considered. 
Additional 
yield 
increases 
from 
the extension 
services 
were 
not 
quantified, 
nor 
were 
the 
potential 
improvements 
in 
the 
quality 
of 
agricultural 
products 
valued. 
4. 
Economic 
rate 
of 
return and

analysis. 
The 
economic 
internal 
rate 
of 
return 
(EIRR) 
and 
Net 
Present 
Value 
(NPV) 
were calculated 
for 
the 
overall project 
and 
for 
the 
irrigation 
improvement and 
groundwater recharge enhancement 
components, 
respectively. 
Project 
benefits were 
calculated 
based 
on 
the assumptions presented 
above. 
Project 
costs 
were 
derived 
from 
the 
software 
COSTAB. 
No 
shadow 
pricing 
for 
foreign 
exchange 
was 
performed, since 
the 
official 
exchange 
rate 
broadly reflect 
the 
equilibrium 
exchange 
rate.

base 
case 
scenario 
indicates the

of

17%

A

constructed 
to

the

considered 
at 
two

depths 
(150 
and 
300 
meters). 
Quantified 
benefits 
from 
irrigation 
improvements were 
assumed 
to 
be: 
(a) 
savings 
in 
pumping 
costs 
(both capital 
and 
O&M) 
as 
a 
result 
of 
improved irrigation 
efficiency; and 
(b) 
agricultural 
yield 
increases, 
as 
observed 
and 
measured 
during 
the 
implementation of 
the 
LWCP. 
Yield 
increases 
were conservatively 
assumed 
at 
2% 
for 
improvement 
of 
existing 
piped 
systems, 
3% 
for 
conversion 
of 
earth canal 
to 
piped-conveyance systems 
and 
4% 
for 
the 
introduction 
of 
modem 
on-farm 
irrigation 
systems. 
It 
is 
assumed 
that water 
devth and 
the 
irrigated 
area 
remain 
constant 
over 
time 
in 
both 
the 
“with” 
and 
“without” 
project 
scenarios. 
A 
well 
serves 
an 
average 
of 
5 
hectares 
and 
10 
households. 
Water  devth 
is 
a 
major 
factor 
in 
the 
determination 
of 
the 
financial 
attractiveness 
of 
irrigation 
improvement. 
Financial 
returns 
substantially 
increase (conversely, cost 
recovery 
period 
decreases) 
with 
the 
depth 
of 
the aquifer. 
Irrigation 
improvements, 
in 
particular 
the 
introduction 
of 
modem 
on-farm 
- 
74 
- 
Page  79
irrigation 
technology, 
are 
more 
likely 
to 
be 
successful 
in 
areas 
with 
deeper 
water tables. 
Under 
current 
price 
structures 
and 
level 
of 
groundwater 
depletion, 
on-farm 
irrigation 
improvements 
is 
only 
justified 
(from 
a 
farmer's 
point 
of view) 
where: 
(a) 
higher-value 
crops 
are 
grown; 
and 
(b) 
water depth 
is 
high. 
It 
is 
anticipated that 
as 
the water tables 
continue 
to 
drop, 
irrigation 
improvements 
will 
become 
more and more 
attractive 
(as 
long 
as 
agriculture 
remains 
financially 
viable). 
From 
a 
farmer's 
point 
of view,  the 
most 
attractive 
activity 
is 
by 
far 
the 
conversion 
of 
earth 
canals 
into 
piped 
system, 
followed 
by 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
piped-conveyance 
system. 
The 
introduction 
of 
on-farm 
modem 
irrigation 
techniques 
appears 
interesting 
on 
a 
financial 
point 
of 
view only 
when 
water 
depth 
is 
high. 
Without 
adequate subsidies, 
significant 
adoption rate 
of 
on-farm 
irrigation 
technology 
appears 
doubtful.

Capital

for

previous

However, they

to

of 
the 
project. Specifically, 
increased 
levels 
of 
project 
subsidies 
to 
participating 
users 
have 
been 
defined 
to: 
(a) 
accelerate 
participation 
in 
the 
first 
phase, 
thereby 
having 
a strong demonstration effect; and 
(b) 
serve 
as 
incentive 
to 
farmers 
to 
refrain 
from 
future 
use 
of 
the saved 
water 
and 
future extension 
of 
irrigated 
areas. 
The 
selected 
levels of 
beneficiaries 
contributions 
to 
capital 
costs 
are 
indicated 
in 
the 
table 
below. 
All 
O&M 
costs 
would 
be 
borne 
by 
the 
direct 
beneficiaries. 
Installation 
cost 
Water 
users' 
share 
would 
be 
paid 
in 
two 
stages: 
(a) 
a 
down-payment 
of 
YR 
20,000 
would 
be 
made 
prior 
to 
design, 
to 
ensure 
farmer's 
commitment 
to 
the 
project; 
(b) 
the 
balance 
would 
be 
paid prior  to 
installation 
of 
the 
equipment. 
Water 
user's 
would 
deposit 
their contribution 
in 
a 
special account 
at 
the 
Sana'a 
branch 
of 
the 
CACB 
which 
should 
notify 
the 
project 
and 
credit 
the 
project's 
account 
from 
the 
deposit 
as 
quickly 
as 
possible. 
It 
is 
foreseen 
that water 
users 
could 
access 
credit 
from 
CACB to 
finance 
their 
share 
of 
the investment 
cost. 
The  project 
would 
support 
this

by 
helping 
water 
users 
in 
the

applications 
and 
providing

all

the proposed 
activities 
to

financed. 
However, 
the 
project 
would not 
finance 
or 
guarantee, 
in 
any 
way, 
the 
farmer's 
share 
of 
investment.

General 
Public 
procurement 
in 
Yemen 
is 
regulated 
by 
the 
principles 
set 
out 
under 
Law 
No. 
3 
of 
1997 
and 
Decree 
No. 
234 
of 
the 
same 
year. 
In 
accordance 
with 
the above 
mentioned 
regulation, 
the 
Apex 
body 
in 
the 
government 
for 
the 
procurement 
function 
is 
the 
Supreme 
Government 
Auctions and 
Tender 
Committee, 
also 
called 
the 
Higher 
Tender 
Board. 
Although 
from 
a 
policy 
perspective, 
the 
base 
line 
for 
future 
reforms 
in 
public 
sector 
procurement 
in 
Yemen 
assumes 
the 
preferred 
method 
of 
govemment 
contracting 
is 
open 
tender, 
nonetheless, 
many 
large 
contracts 
are 
in 
fact 
split 
into 
smaller 
contracts 
that 
fall 
under 
the 
threshold 
of 
the 
HTB’s 
due 
diligence. 
Overall, 
while 
the 
1997 
Law 
and 
regulations 
are 
an 
improvement, 
the 
view 
of 
staff 
working 
on 
Yemen 
is 
they 
are 
not 
applied 
with 
the 
degree 
of 
commitment 
necessary 
to 
reduce 
the prevalent 
tendency 
for 
inefficient 
and uneconomic 
procurement. 
A 
CPAR 
was 
finalized  and 
issued 
in 
December 
2000. 
The 
CPAR 
has 
noted 
the  current 
legal 
framework 
for 
public 
procurement,  however, 
falls 
short 
of 
acceptable 
international 
procurement 
standards 
in 
the 
following key 
areas, 
namely: 
the 
lack 
of 
Standard 
Bid 
Documents 
(SBDs), 
gaps 
in 
the legislation, 
and 
that 
parts 
of 
even 
the 
current 
legislation 
do 
still 
conflict 
with 
Bank 
guidelines. 
Although, 
in 
the 
long 
term, 
it 
is 
considered essential that 
these 
gaps 
in 
the 
current 
legislation need 
to 
be 
addressed 
through 
a 
revised framework 
requiring 
Parliament’s  endorsement. Nonetheless, 
for 
the 
short 
term 
with 
support 
from 
an 
IDF 
grant 
for 
procurement 
reforms,  a comprehensive 
National 
Procurement 
Manual 
with 
proposed SBDs 
has 
recently 
been 
prepared 
and 
is 
currently 
under 
review 
by 
the 
Yemeni 
authorities.  Once 
approved 
and 
issued, 
as 
well 
as 
actual 
implementation 
of 
planned 
workshops 
by 
the consultants 
in 
the 
next 
few 
months, the manuals 
for 
public 
sector 
procurement 
with 
national 
application, 
are 
expected 
to 
significantly 
enhance 
the 
potential 
for 
realizing 
the 
objectives 
of 
economy 
and 
efficiency, 
as 
well 
as 
an 
increased 
level 
of 
GOY 
compliance 
with 
IDA 
guidelines.

of 
Bank 
Guidelines

publication 
of 
the 
frst 
Specific 
Procurement 
Notice 
(SPN), 
and bidders 
who 
have expressed 
an 
interest 
would 
be 
notified directly 
by 
the 
PMU, 
The 
GPN 
will 
be 
updated 
on 
a 
yearly 
basis 
and 
will 
show 
all 
outstanding 
ICB 
contracts for goods 
and 
all 
large 
consulting 
contracts. SPNs 
for 
goods 
to 
be 
procured 
under 
ICB 
and 
Expressions 
of 
Interest 
(EOIs) 
for 
consultant 
services estimated 
to 
cost 
the 
equivalent 
of 
US$200,000 
or 
more 
will 
be 
advertised 
in 
international 
newspaper 
and 
in 
the 
UNDB. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
assignments estimated 
to 
cost 
US$lOO,OOO 
or 
less, 
the assignment 
may 
be 
advertised 
nationally 
and 
the 
shortlist 
may 
be 
made 
up 
entirely 
of 
national 
consultants 
provided 
that at 
least 
three 
qualified 
national 
firms 
or 
individuals 
are 
available 
in 
the country, 
and 
foreign 
consultants 
who 
wish to 
participate 
are 
not 
excluded 
from 
consideration.

contractors 
competing 
under 
ICB 
for 
civil 
works 
contracts 
would 
receive 
a preference 
in 
bid

of 
7.5 
percent.

Preference 
would 
be 
applied 
in 
accordance 
with 
IDA 
Guidelines 
for 
Procurement, 
Procurement Planning. 
The 
general 
absence 
of 
procurement 
planning 
in 
most 
sectors 
in 
Yemen 
has 
led to 
inefficient 
project 
implementation. 
A 
draft 
Overall 
Procurement 
Plan 
(OPP) 
for 
goods 
and 
equipment, 
works  and 
consultant 
services 
has 
been 
reviewed 
and 
finalized 
at  negotiations. 
In 
addition, 
prior to 
Board 
presentation, a 
Detailed 
Procurement 
Plan 
(DPP) 
indicating 
the procurement 
method 
and 
processing 
time 
for 
each 
contract 
has 
been  prepared 
covering 
the 
first 
year 
of 
implementation 
(attached). 
An 
updated procurement 
plan 
will 
be submitted 
to 
IDA 
for 
review 
during 
every 
supervision 
mission. 
The 
progress 
reports 
forwarded 
to 
IDA 
will 
report 
on 
the procurement 
activities 
in 
an 
appropriate 
degree 
of 
detail. 
Procurement 
Implementation 
Arrangements. 
A 
Project 
Steering 
Committee 
has 
been 
set 
up, 
chaired 
by 
the 
MOPD, 
with 
representatives 
from 
NWRA, 
NWSA, 
MAI 
and 
ACU. 
This 
committee 
would 
oversee 
project 
preparation  and 
implementation 
(Phase 
I 
of  the 
AF'C), 
as 
well 
as 
the 
finalization 
of 
the 
basin 
management plan. 
A 
TS-SBC 
set 
up 
to 
manage 
the 
proposed 
project 
will 
also have 
the 
responsibility 
to 
out 
procurement 
aspects 
of 
the proposed 
operation, 
The 
establishment 
and 
staffing 
of 
the  TS-SBC 
would 
be 
a 
Condition 
of 
Effectiveness. 
The 
TS-SBC 
would 
have 
operating 
authority, 
in 
line 
with 
the 
directives 
agreed 
between 
GOY 
and 
IDA. 
Key 
responsibilities 
of 
the  TS-SBC 
would 
include  project 
preparation 
and 
implementation, 
financial 
management, 
procurement, disbursement, 
and 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
of 
CDD 
sub-projects. 
The 
TS-SBC 
is 
expected 
to 
consist 
of 
four  full-time 
professionals 
(a 
water 
resource 
specialistlhydrogeologist, 
a 
sociologist, 
procurement 
specialist 
and 
an 
accountantladministrator), 
and 
other 
short-term 
- 
80 
- 
Page  85
consultants 
as 
needed. 
Consulting 
firms 
or 
individuals 
will 
be 
contracted, 
as 
needed, 
to 
prepare 
engineering 
design, 
specification, 
and 
bidding 
documents, 
and 
also 
to 
provide  construction 
supervision, 
technical 
advice 
and 
assistance 
to 
communities 
during 
the construction 
of 
the 
CDD 
sub-projects. 
Capacity 
Assessment 
A 
formal 
procurement capacity 
assessment 
of 
the 
TS-SBC, 
which 
remains 
to 
be 
established, 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
prior 
to 
Credit 
Effectiveness 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Procurement Services 
Policy 
Group 
(OPCPR) 
Guidelines 
dated 
July 
15,2002. 
The 
project 
is 
considered 
high 
risk 
since the 
TS-SBC 
will 
be 
in 
constant dialogue 
with 
a 
number 
of 
stakeholders spread 
over 
key 
ministries. 
The 
assessment 
will 
outline 
the 
main 
issues 
and 
recommendations, 
and 
an 
action 
plan 
will 
also 
be prepared 
and 
agreed 
upon 
with 
the SBC 
which 
will 
focus 
on: 
(a) 
establishing 
procurement 
capacity 
in 
the TS-SBC 
through 
timely 
recruitment 
of 
one 
or 
perhaps 
two 
procurement 
specialists, 
provision 
of 
adequate 
training,  guidance 
and 
external  technical  audits  specific 
to 
procurement  (model 
TORS 
for 
the 
procurement 
specialists 
would 
be 
shared 
with 
TS-SBC  at 
negotiations) 
by 
consultants; 
and 
(b) 
logistical 
and 
material 
support 
for 
proper 
filing 
of 
procurement documentation 
where 
required. 
An 
Action 
Plan 
will 
also 
be 
developed 
and 
agreed 
upon 
for 
community-based 
procurement 
activities 
at 
the 
district 
and 
local 
levels 
using 
the 
Bank's 
Fiduciary 
Management 
for 
CDD 
projects 
of 
May 
2000 
as 
a 
reference 
guide. 
In 
particular,  adoption 
of 
Project Implementation 
Manual 
(PIM) 
in 
which 
simplified 
procurement 
processes 
for  sub-project proposals 
from 
community-based  groups 
is 
made 
part 
of 
the 
PIM, 
and 
determined satisfactory 
to 
IDA 
will 
be 
a 
condition 
of 
Effectiveness. 
In 
the 
interim, 
pending 
the scheduled 
capacity 
assessment 
of 
TS-SBC, 
the 
prior 
review 
thresholds 
in

DCA 
have been 
set 
on

the 
risk

(high) 
of 
a 
similar

in 
Yemen's

the

Improvement Project 
(P062714). 
Procurement 
Methods 
Civil 
Works. 
The 
total 
cost 
of 
works 
is 
estimated 
to 
cost US$8.50 
million. 
It 
would 
cover 
works, 
including 
supply 
and 
installation 
of 
pumps, 
piped 
and 
pressurized 
irrigation 
systems, 
rehabilitation 
of 
existing 
dams 
and 
construction 
of 
new 
dams, 
gabions,  spate breakers, 
wadi 
spurs 
and 
protection 
works, 
land 
leveling 
and 
wastewater treatment 
plant 
improvements. 
Bid 
packages, 
with 
an 
estimated 
contract value 
above US$2.0 
million 
equivalent,

be procured 
using 
ICB 
procedures 
and 
documents 
and 
advertised 
in 
UNDB 
and 
in 
local 
newspapers.

value 
below 
US$2.0

up 
to 
US$S.O 
million 
in 
aggregate, 
would 
be 
procured 
using 
NCB 
procedures 
and 
documents 
and 
advertised 
in 
at 
least 
two 
local 
newspapers. 
NCB 
contracts 
would 
be  open 
to 
eligible  foreign 
bidders 
if 
they 
wish 
to 
participate.

and

below

.O

lowest 
price 
quotation 
for 
the 
required 
work, 
and 
who 
has 
the experience 
and 
resources 
to 
complete the contract 
successfully.

by

from

for

and

(SA) 
to

should be

of 
expenditures 
by 
TS-SBC. 
Basically,

process 
before

each 
year, 
every 
participating 
agency 
will

and

an 
annual

with 
the SBC.

Payment Procedures 
Payments 
to 
contractors 
and 
third 
parties 
from 
the 
SA 
will 
follow 
the 
internal 
control 
procedures 
stipulated 
in 
the 
FM 
manual, 
and 
will 
include 
the 
following 
steps: 
(i) 
The 
project financial 
officer 
ensures 
that 
all 
supporting 
payment 
documents are available, 
and verifies them 
for 
completeness 
and 
accuracy. 
The financial 
officer 
then 
prepares 
and 
signs 
a Payment 
Order 
(PO) 
and 
forwards 
it, 
along 
with 
the 
supporting 
documents, 
to 
the 
project 
manager 
for 
review 
and 
signature. 
(ii) 
The 
financial officer 
prepares 
and 
posts 
appropriate 
journal 
accounting 
entries 
in 
the 
general 
ledger 
after 
the 
Project 
Manager 
approves payment. 
(iii)The  PO 
and 
all 
supporting 
documents 
are 
then 
sent 
to 
MOPD, 
which 
verifies 
that  the 
procurement  process was 
conducted 
in 
accordance 
with 
agreed procedures 
in 
the 
Legal 
Agreement. 
MOPD 
also 
maintains 
separate 
Statements 
of 
Account 
for 
all 
projects financed 
by 
foreign 
donors. 
Accordingly, 
the amount 
of 
payment 
is 
entered 
into 
the 
related 
project 
- 
87 
- 
Page  92
statement account. 
MOF 
an 
approval 
letter 
acknowledging 
that 
they 
have 
reviewed 
the 
PO 
and 
are 
approving 
it 
for 
payment. 
(v) 
The 
MOF 
then 
conducts 
its 
own 
review 
of

PO 
and supporting

If

they

Records 
Management 
The 
management 
of 
financial 
records 
should provide 
a 
paper 
trail 
on 
which 
the 
accounting 
system 
is 
based, 
and 
verification 
of 
financial 
transactions. 
For 
each 
financial 
transaction,  a 
separate 
record 
should 
be 
created. 
Generally, records management 
should 
include: 
(i) 
clearly 
defined 
procedures 
for 
creating 
and 
maintaining 
records; 
(ii) 
adequate 
backup 
arrangements; 
(iii) 
storage 
of 
financial 
records 
under 
lock 
and 
key, 
and 
safe 
from 
environmental 
risks 
(fire 
and 
(iv) 
easy 
access 
for 
authorized personnel only. 
water 
damage); 
and 
All 
project 
documents 
will 
be maintained 
at 
the 
TS-SBC 
during 
project implementation and 
for 
one 
year 
after 
the project’s 
closing 
date. 
All 
project 
documents 
will 
be 
provided 
to 
the 
project’s auditor 
and 
to 
visiting 
IDA 
supervision 
missions. 
Financial 
Monitoring 
ReDorts 
{FMRsj 
It 
is 
envisaged 
that 
FMR 
will 
be 
produced 
semi-annually 
rather 
than 
quarterly. 
The implementation 
of 
the 
two 
main 
components 
of 
the 
project 
(demand management 
and 
supply 
management, 
$20 
million) 
represents 
items 
of 
seasonal 
nature, 
and 
quarterly 
monitoring 
reports 
seem  excessive. 
According 
to 
the nature 
of 
the 
project 
and 
discussions 
with 
stakeholders, 
FMR 
will 
be 
produced and 
sent 
to 
the 
Bank 
un-audited 
within 
45 
days 
of 
each 
semester-end (June 
and 
December). 
The 
reports 
will 
show: 
(a) 
summary progress 
report 
on 
project 
implementation; 
(b) 
cost 
per 
component 
for 
the 
quarter 
and 
cumulative; 
(c) 
costs 
incurred per 
expenditures 
type; 
and 
(d) 
procurement 
incurred 
on 
contracts 
below 
the 
stated threshold. 
The 
chart 
of 
accounts 
for 
the 
project 
will 
be 
set 
up 
to 
facilitate 
the automatic generation 
of 
the 
FMR. 
The 
contents 
and format 
were agreed 
upon 
during 
negotiations. 
In 
Phase 
II 
and 
III, 
MAI 
will 
prepare, 
on 
quarterly 
basis, 
summary 
reports 
on 
its 
project 
related 
activities showing 
all 
project 
expenditures 
incurred 
from 
all 
resources 
and 
a 
summary 
of 
physical 
progress achieved. 
These 
quarterly reports 
should be 
sent 
to 
SBC and 
IDA 
within 
30 
days 
after 
the 
end 
of 
each quarter.

Auditing 
The project 
records

(project

The auditor

prior 
to 
project 
effectiveness. 
Prior 
written 
approval 
(“no 
objection”) 
of 
IDA 
on 
the 
auditor 
is 
mandatory. 
The 
audit 
will 
cover 
all 
sources 
of 
funds 
related 
to 
the 
project including 
IDA 
credit. 
Also, 
it 
will 
cover 
all 
expenditures 
incurred  including 
those 
under 
SOEs 
and 
their 
compliance 
with 
the 
Development 
Credit 
Agreement. 
The 
auditor 
will 
issue 
an 
opinion 
on 
the 
fairness 
of 
the 
project 
financial 
statements 
and 
a 
separate 
opinion 
on 
the 
eligibility 
of 
all 
expenditures 
incurred 
and 
claimed 
under 
SOEs. 
The 
audit 
report should be 
sent 
to 
IDA 
no 
later than 
four 
months 
of 
year-end. 
Due 
to 
project 
complex 
arrangements 
and 
new 
establishment 
of 
SBC 
and 
TS-SBC, 
the 
FM 
risk 
related 
to 
the 
project 
is 
considered 
high. 
This 
consideration 
was made 
with 
the 
recognition 
of 
no 
FM 
staff 
on 
board, 
lack 
of 
FM 
system, 
and 
inclusion 
of 
beneficiary 
contributions. 
These 
risks 
will 
be 
mitigated 
through: 
(a) 
allocation 
of 
funds 
for 
the 
FM 
system 
and 
the 
financial 
officer 
costs; 
(b) 
having 
adequate 
FM 
system 
and 
appointment 
of 
a 
qualified  financial 
officer 
as 
a 
condition 
of 
effectiveness; 
and 
(c) 
close 
project 
supervision 
by 
the 
IDA 
team 
and 
the 
FM 
specialist 
in 
the 
Country 
Office, 
which 
will 
allow 
building 
capacity 
within 
TS-SBC 
for 
proper 
implementation.  Supervision 
and missions 
for the 
following 
years 
will 
be 
determined 
based 
on 
satisfactory performance 
in 
the 
first 
year. 
Stafing

staff 
of

Rural

records),

project 
chart 
of 
accounts, 
and

local 
accounting

on 
IDA 
FM

training 
from

Additional 
Annex 
11 
: 
Resettlement Policy Framework 
REPUBLIC 
OF YEMEN: 
Sana’a 
Basin Water Management 
Project 
SANA’A 
BASIN 
WATER 
MANAGEMENT 
PROJECT 
Resettlement 
Policy 
Framework 
Project 
Description 
1. 
The 
Sana’a 
Basin 
is 
located 
in 
central highlands 
of 
Yemen 
and 
includes 
the 
Capital 
City 
Sana’a. 
The 
basin 
has 
an 
area 
of 
3,200 
km2 
and 
an 
average 
elevation 
of 
2,200 
meters above 
mean 
sea 
level. 
Current 
population 
of 
the 
basin 
is 
estimated 
at 
about 
1.5 
million, 
of 
which 
300,000 
live 
in 
the 
rural 
areas, 
and 
the 
population growth 
rate 
is 
projected 
to 
decline 
from 
the current 
6% 
to 
about 
4% 
by 
2030. 
The 
basin 
includes 
some  110,000 
hectares 
(ha) 
of 
arable 
land, 
of 
which 
about 
20,000 
ha 
are 
irrigated. 
Groundwater 
is 
the 
mejor 
source 
of 
water 
in 
the basin. 
Abstractions 
began 
to 
exceed recharge 
during 
the 
mid-1980s 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
the 
rapid 
urban 
population 
growth 
and 
a 
sharp 
increase 
in 
water 
use 
by 
agriculture, 
encouraged 
by 
the 
introduction 
of 
tube-well 
technologies. 
The 
basin 
is 
experiencing 
a serious 
depletion 
of 
groundwater 
resources 
with 
an 
associated 
degradation 
in 
water 
quality. 
In 
addition, 
a 
new 
Waste 
Water 
Treatment  Plant 
(WWTP) 
has 
recently 
been commissioned 
to 
the 
north 
of 
Sana’a, 
its 
effluent 
could provide 
a 
valuable water 
resource 
if 
public 
health 
issues 
can 
be 
adequately 
addressed. 
2. 
To 
address 
the water scarcity 
issues, 
the Government 
and 
the 
World 
Bank 
have 
agreed 
to 
prepare 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin 
Water 
Management Program. 
The International 
Development 
Association 
(IDA) 
will 
implement 
the 
Program through 
a  15-year 
Adaptable 
Program Credit 
(APC) 
financed. 
The 
broad 
objectives 
of 
this 
proposed 
program 
are: 
(a) 
to 
increase the 
efficiency 
of 
agricultural 
water 
use 
within 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin; 
and 
(b) 
to 
accelerate 
aquifer 
recharge 
to allow for 
a gradual 
shift 
to 
a less water-based 
rural 
economy. 
The 
objectives 
of 
the 
Phase 
I, 
five-year 
pilot 
project 
of 
this 
Program 
are: 
(a) 
to 
test 
and 
develop 
demand 
and 
supply 
management methods 
on 
a 
limited 
scale, 
so 
as 
to 
apply 
them 
on 
a large-scale 
throughout 
the 
basin 
during 
the 
subsequent phases 
of 
the 
program; 
(b) 
to 
establish the regulatory, 
legal 
and 
institutional 
framework 
needed for 
more 
sustainable 
water 
resources management 
in 
the 
basin; 
and 
(c) 
to 
carry out 
the 
preparation 
of 
the 
Phase 
IT 
project 
of 
the Program. 
3. 
The 
program involves many 
sub-projects, 
which 
have 
not 
been defined 
and 
detailed 
at 
this 
time, 
among 
which 
are 
physical 
works. 
In 
the 
first 
phase 
of 
this 
program, the 
rehabilitation 
of 
existing 
dams 
and 
the 
construction 
of 
five new 
dams 
are 
planned. 
It 
is 
not 
known 
at 
this 
time 
whether 
there 
will 
be 
permanent displacement 
or 
loss 
of 
assets 
among 
the 
population 
of 
owners 
and 
users, 
and 
to 
this 
end 
this 
Resettlement 
Policy 
Framework 
has  been 
developed 
to 
ensure 
that 
all 
the 
physical  works 
are 
in 
conformity 
with 
Bank 
policy. 
The 
land 
required 
for 
these 
works 
already 
lies 
either 
within 
the 
public 
domain, 
or 
will 
be 
acquired 
by 
following 
a process 
of 
expropriation and 
compensation. 
4. 
The  project’s 
design 
has 
taken 
care 
to 
avoid 
displacement,  dispossession 
and 
conflicts. 
Nonetheless,  the 
construction 
of 
new 
dams 
still 
might 
entail 
some 
unforeseen 
land 
acquisition 
and 
compensation 
issues, 
in 
which 
case 
OPlBP 
4.12 
related 
to 
Involuntary 
Resettlement 
will 
be 
triggered. 
This 
Resettlement 
Policy 
Framework 
(RPF) 
serves 
to 
show the procedures 
to 
be 
followed 
by 
GOY 
to 
compensate 
for 
assets 
taken 
or 
loss 
of 
access 
to 
livelihood 
resources, 
the 
procedures 
for 
compensation, 
and the 
measures 
to 
mitigate 
any 
negative 
impacts. 
- 
103 
- 
Page  108
Principles 
and 
Objectives 
Governing 
Resettlement 
Preparation 
and 
Implementation 
5. 
This 
Resettlement 
Policy 
Framework 
establishes 
the 
procedures 
for 
any 
land 
acquisition 
or 
loss 
or 
restriction 
of 
access 
to 
assets, 
through expropriation 
in 
the 
public 
interest. 
It 
applies 
to 
private 
and 
communal 
lands 
or 
properties 
affected 
throughout 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin 
Water 
Management 
Project. 
In 
particular, 
it 
addresses 
how 
the 
GOY 
will 
identify, 
mitigate 
and 
compensate 
any 
adverse 
impacts 
on 
the 
assets 
and 
livelihoods 
of 
the people 
directly 
affected 
by 
possible 
expropriations. 
It 
is 
intended 
to 
safeguard the interests 
of 
the 
population 
impacted 
by 
the 
project, especially the 
poor 
and 
vulnerable. 
6. 
This 
Framework 
also describes the 
required 
organizational 
arrangements 
and 
design 
criteria 
to 
be 
applied 
to 
sub-projects 
during 
project 
implementation 
and 
the 
procedures 
and 
content 
of 
any 
abbreviated 
or 
full 
resettlement 
action 
plans 
if 
and 
as 
required. 
7. 
The 
objective 
of 
the 
Bank’s 
policy 
is 
to 
ensure that 
throughout 
its 
life, 
the 
project 
fully 
complies 
with 
the 
principle 
that 
any 
involuntary 
loss 
of 
assets 
or 
relocation 
of 
economic 
activities 
or 
residence, 
are 
minimized 
and 
fully 
compensated, 
and 
that 
adequate 
procedures 
exist 
for 
prior 
consultation 
of 
all 
affected 
persons 
For 
the 
purposes 
of 
this 
framework, “affected 
persons” 
are 
defined 
as 
all 
persons who, 
as 
a 
result 
of 
works carried 
out 
or 
to 
be 
carried out 
under the 
Project, 
would 
incur 
involuntary 
loss, 
temporarily 
or 
permanently, 
of 
land, 
shelter, 
productive 
assets 
or 
access 
to 
productive 
assets, 
or 
of 
income 
or 
means 
of 
livelihood 
and, 
as 
a 
consequence, 
would 
have 
their 
living 
standards 
or 
production 
levels 
adversely affected, 
assessment 
of 
losses 
and 
entitlements, 
handling 
complaints 
and 
disputes, 
and 
monitoring 
the 
outcomes. 
In 
particular, 
it 
provides 
that 
the 
outcomes 
conform 
to 
the 
principles 
of 
full 
and 
prior 
compensation 
for 
any 
lost 
assets 
and 
full 
restoration 
of 
standards 
of 
living 
that 
are 
directly 
and 
adversely  affected. 
This 
policy 
complies 
with 
the 
Bank’s 
safeguards 
requirements 
for 
financing 
investment 
projects 
as 
detailed 
in 
Operational 
Policy 
4.12. 
The 
policy 
also 
applies 
to 
those 
who 
lack 
legal 
or 
formal 
ownership 
of 
affected 
assets 
and 
are 
entitled 
to 
fair 
compensation 
and 
all 
other 
forms 
of 
assistance 
(housing, 
social 
services 
etc). 
8. 
This 
policy 
provides 
for 
any situations that 
may 
arise 
where the need 
for 
temporary 
or 
permanent 
additional 
land 
becomes apparent 
at 
the 
stage 
of 
sub-project 
selection 
or 
technical 
design, 
or 
where 
execution 
of 
physical works 
results 
in 
the disturbance 
of 
occupants 
or 
users. 
Legal 
Framework 
9. 
Yemen’s 
legislation provides 
the 
legal 
framework 
for 
this 
RPF. 
The 
conditions 
and 
provisions 
for 
declaring 
areas 
of 
land 
as 
public 
eminent 
domain; 
the 
provisions 
for 
expropriation 
and 
compensation; 
and 
the 
definition 
of 
rights 
of 
owners 
and 
users 
of 
communal 
property 
all 
exist 
in 
law. 
However, 
because 
of 
the 
weakness 
of 
the 
judicial 
system 
and 
the 
presence 
and prevalence 
of 
tribal 
law, 
it 
is 
necessary that 
the 
RPF 
take the 
social context 
into 
account 
and 
adapt 
it 
to 
local 
conditions. 
Laws Goveming 
Propertv 
10. 
The 
Yemeni 
Constitution (Article 
20) 
protects citizens 
from 
the 
general 
expropriation 
of 
their 
assets. 
Public 
Eminent 
Domain 
Law 
(PEDL), 
passed 
by 
parliament 
in 
1995, 
gives governmental bodies 
(including 
governors) the 
right 
to 
acquire 
private property 
for 
projects 
in 
the 
public 
interest. 
Yemeni 
law 
stresses 
that 
land 
expropriation 
is 
to 
take 
place 
only 
in 
the 
event 
that 
no 
suitable 
land 
alternatives 
exist 
that 
are 
already 
in 
the 
public 
domain. 
In 
such 
instances, 
the 
legal 
provisions 
for 
expropriation 
and 
compensation 
will 
apply. 
The 
law 
describes: 
(i) 
situations 
of 
legal 
expropriation; 
(ii) 
procedures 
for 
expropriation; 
(iii) 
the 
agency 
responsible 
for 
valuation 
of 
the compensation 
and 
its 
procedures. 
- 
104- 
Page  109
11. 
Three 
main 
types 
of 
expropriation 
procedures 
described 
in 
the 
PEDL 
apply to 
this project: 
(i) 
Judicial  Expropriation 
where  the 
state 
declares the  need 
for 
the 
law 
based 
on 
public 
interest; 
(ii) 
Amicable 
Expropriation 
is 
based 
on 
the 
consent 
of 
the 
owners 
and 
compensation 
is 
to 
be valued 
by 
an 
Estimation 
Committee; 
(iii) 
Temporary Appropriation 
is 
when 
governmental 
authorities 
require 
access 
to 
lands 
for 
construction of 
public 
works. 
In 
such 
cases, 
the 
law 
requires 
that 
it 
be returned 
to 
the 
appropriate 
owner 
in 
the 
same 
condition 
that 
it 
was received, 
and the 
implementing 
government agency 
is 
required 
to 
compensate the 
owner 
for 
any 
damages. 
The Evaluation 
Committee 
will 
estimate 
the value 
of 
the 
damages. 
12. 
Article 
59 
in 
the 
State’s 
Real 
Estates 
Law 
also recognizes 
the 
rights 
of 
squatters 
on 
public 
domain 
to 
receive 
compensation 
in 
the event 
of 
involuntary 
settlement 
according 
to 
the 
standards 
set 
by 
the 
technical 
committees 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
and 
Irrigation. 
Laws Govemine 
Participation 
13. 
The 
Yemeni 
Constitution, 
Elections 
Law 
and 
Local 
Administration 
Law 
recognize  the 
importance 
of 
communal participation, 
although 
there 
are 
no 
specific 
procedures 
and guidelines 
for 
public

Land 
Acauisition 
Plan 
(LAP) 
Procedures 
17. 
Where 
impacts 
would 
be 
unavoidable, 
Technical 
Secretariat 
of

Sana’a 
Basin 
Commission 
(TS-SBC)

by

Land

for

and 
the

provided 
by

pre-feasibility 
studies. 
This 
Land 
Acquisition

scientists’ 
findings, 
will

and 
justify 
the 
tempomy or

proposed,

on

who 
lack legal 
or 
formal 
ownership 
of 
affected 
assets.

must 
be included 
in

Land Acquisition 
Plan 
after

in

carried out

Abbreviated 
Resettlement 
Plan 
Procedures 
19. 
Given 
the 
nature 
of 
the 
proposed 
sub-project 
components, 
any 
activity 
that 
may 
cause 
taking of 
land 
or 
other 
assets 
is 
likely to 
affect 
relatively 
few 
people 
(e,g., 
less 
than 
200 
people), 
and 
impacts 
are 
likely to 
be 
relatively minor 
(e.g., 
not 
requiring 
changes 
in 
occupation 
or 
relocation 
of 
residence). 
Under 
such  circumstances, 
an 
abbreviated 
resettlement 
plan 
will 
be 
prepared 
for 
each  sub-project.

and

the persons

(including

of 
impacts 
extent 
of 
that 
acquisition, 
including 
sketch 
maps; 
rights) 
and 
the 
types 
of 
impact 
(Census); 
and 
a corresponding table 
of 
compensation entitlements 
and 
the 
nature 
and 
bases 
of 
compensation

(iv) 
Consultation 
with 
displaced people 
about acceptable choices 
and 
alternatives; 
(v)

(vi)

22.

Consultation

Grievance 
Procedures 
26. 
Regardless 
of 
the 
issues 
of 
land 
acquisition 
or 
loss 
of 
assets, 
in 
the 
Sana’a 
Basin 
Project 
consultation 
with 
the  beneficiaries  before 
the  design 
of 
any 
sub-projects 
is 
of 
critical 
importance. 
Because 
water 
is 
a common 
asset 
subject 
to 
tribal 
regulations,  the arrangements 
and 
agreements 
for 
the 
use 
of 
water 
from 
any 
dams 
must 
be 
settled 
before 
any 
sub-projects 
are 
developed 
and approved.  There 
are 
instances 
where water 
from 
full 
dams 
cannot 
be 
used 
because 
of 
local 
disputes 
over 
water, 
thus 
the 
social 
analysis 
of 
the 
customary 
water 
tenancy 
laws 
must 
be 
clear 
and 
agreements 
established 
before 
any 
sub-project 
is 
approved. 
27. 
Where 
there 
will 
be 
any 
land

or

of 
access 
to 
resources 
and 
either abbreviated 
or 
full 
RAP 
is 
required, 
early 
consultations

constitute 
part

Following

Information

(i)

28. 
If 
project-affected 
persons 
are 
not 
satisfied 
with 
proposed

arrangements, 
or

with 
actual

implementation, 
they 
can 
first 
seek 
satisfaction 
through 
local

for 
resolving conflict. 
If

does 
not 
result 
in 
resolution 
of 
issues, 
project-affected 
persons 
will 
direct their 
grievances 
to 
the special grievance 
committee 
to 
be 
formed 
within 
TS-SBC. 
The 
grievance 
committee 
should 
record 
receipt 
of 
grievances 
and 
reply 
to 
the 
project-affected 
person 
or 
persons 
within 
ten 
days 
after 
receiving 
the 
grievances. 
If 
the grievances 
or 
disputes 
cannot 
be 
resolved 
through administrative 
action,  the 
project-affected  person 
can 
initiate legal 
proceedings 
in 
accordance 
with 
provincial 
and national 
law.

and

other 
costs 
linked 
to 
resettlement 
(e.g. 
costs 
associated 
with 
housing, 
any 
related technical 
assistance, 
monitoring, 
etc.). 
Monitoring 
and 
Evaluation 
35. 
The 
requirements 
for 
monitoring 
and  evaluation 
as 
outlined 
here 
will 
be 
incorporated 
into 
whichever resettlement instrument 
is 
required 
(abbreviated 
or 
fill 
RAP). 
The 
primary 
responsibility 
for 
monitoring 
the 
implementation 
of 
resettlement 
measures 
will 
be 
with 
TS-SBC. 
Depending 
on 
the 
degree 
of 
complexity 
of 
the 
required 
measures 
for 
land 
acquisition 
and 
resettlement, several agencies 
may 
be 
involved 
in 
this 
process, 
and 
TS-SBC 
will 
monitor 
them 
all 
as 
well 
as 
coordinate the 
execution 
of 
the 
process. 
External monitoring 
agencies 
will 
also 
be 
contracted 
to 
ensure 
that 
the resettlement process 
is 
fair, 
transparent, 
and 
complete. 
Specific 
monitoring 
indicators 
will 
include 
measures 
of 
inputs, 
outputs, 
outcomes 
and 
impacts 
against 
which 
the resettlement process 
will 
be 
measured. 
Further 
details 
of 
the 
monitoring 
mechanisms 
will 
be detailed 
in 
the Resettlement Procedures 
Manual. The 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
of 
this 
component 
of 
the 
project 
will 
also 
be 
undertaken 
during 
Bank 
supervision missions 
with 
qualified social 
scientists 
participating.
